<p><b>Latin</b> (<span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">lingua Latīna</i></span>, <small></small><span class="IPA" lang="la-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Latin" title="Help:IPA/Latin">[ˈlɪŋɡʷa laˈtiːna]</a></span> or <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">Latīnum</i></span>, <small></small><span class="IPA" lang="la-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Latin" title="Help:IPA/Latin">[laˈtiːnʊ̃]</a></span>) is a <a href="/wiki/Classical_language" title="Classical language">classical language</a> belonging to the <a href="/wiki/Italic_languages" title="Italic languages">Italic branch</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Indo-European_languages" title="Indo-European languages">Indo-European languages</a>. Latin was originally a <a href="/wiki/Dialect" title="Dialect">dialect</a> spoken in the lower <a href="/wiki/Tiber" title="Tiber">Tiber</a> area (then known as <a href="/wiki/Latium" title="Latium">Latium</a>) around present-day <a href="/wiki/Rome" title="Rome">Rome</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> but through the power of the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Republic" title="Roman Republic">Roman Republic</a> it became the dominant language in the <a href="/wiki/Italy_(geographical_region)" title="Italy (geographical region)">Italian region</a> and subsequently throughout the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman Empire</a>. Even after the <a href="/wiki/Fall_of_the_Western_Roman_Empire" title="Fall of the Western Roman Empire">fall of Western Rome</a>, Latin remained the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Common_language" title="Common language">common language</a> of <a href="/wiki/International_communication" title="International communication">international communication</a>, science, scholarship and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Academia" title="Academia">academia</a> in Europe until well into the 18th century, when other regional vernaculars (including its own descendants, the <a href="/wiki/Romance_languages" title="Romance languages">Romance languages</a>) supplanted it in common academic and political usage, and it eventually became a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dead_language" title="Dead language">dead language</a> in the modern linguistic definition.
</p><p>Latin is a <a href="/wiki/Fusional_language" title="Fusional language">highly inflected language</a>, with three distinct <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_gender" title="Grammatical gender">genders</a>, six or seven <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_case" title="Grammatical case">noun cases</a>, five declensions, four <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_conjugation" title="Grammatical conjugation">verb conjugations</a>, six <a href="/wiki/Latin_tenses" title="Latin tenses">tenses</a>, three <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_person" title="Grammatical person">persons</a>, three <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_mood" title="Grammatical mood">moods</a>, two <a href="/wiki/Voice_(grammar)" title="Voice (grammar)">voices</a>, two or three <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_aspect" title="Grammatical aspect">aspects</a>, and two <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_number" title="Grammatical number">numbers</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Latin_alphabet" title="Latin alphabet">Latin alphabet</a> is directly derived from the <a href="/wiki/Etruscan_alphabet" title="Etruscan alphabet">Etruscan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Greek_alphabet" title="Greek alphabet">Greek alphabets</a>.
</p><p>By the late <a href="/wiki/Roman_Republic" title="Roman Republic">Roman Republic</a> (75 BC), <a href="/wiki/Old_Latin" title="Old Latin">Old Latin</a> had been standardised into <a href="/wiki/Classical_Latin" title="Classical Latin">Classical Latin</a> used by educated elites. <a href="/wiki/Vulgar_Latin" title="Vulgar Latin">Vulgar Latin</a> was the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Colloquial_language" title="Colloquial language">colloquial form</a> spoken at that time among lower-class <a href="/wiki/Commoner" title="Commoner">commoners</a> and attested in inscriptions and the works of comic playwrights like <a href="/wiki/Plautus" title="Plautus">Plautus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Terence" title="Terence">Terence</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> and author <a href="/wiki/Petronius" title="Petronius">Petronius</a>. <a href="/wiki/Late_Latin" title="Late Latin">Late Latin</a> is the written language from the 3rd century and its various Vulgar Latin dialects developed in the 6th to 9th centuries into the modern <a href="/wiki/Romance_languages" title="Romance languages">Romance languages</a>. <a href="/wiki/Medieval_Latin" title="Medieval Latin">Medieval Latin</a> was used during the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a> as a literary language from the 9th century to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Renaissance" title="The Renaissance">the Renaissance</a>, which then used <a href="/wiki/Renaissance_Latin" title="Renaissance Latin">Renaissance Latin</a>. Later, <a href="/wiki/New_Latin" title="New Latin">New Latin</a> evolved during the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Early_modern_era" title="Early modern era">early modern era</a> to eventually become various forms of rarely spoken <a href="/wiki/Contemporary_Latin" title="Contemporary Latin">Contemporary Latin</a>, one of which, <a href="/wiki/Ecclesiastical_Latin" title="Ecclesiastical Latin">Ecclesiastical Latin</a>, remains the <a href="/wiki/Official_language" title="Official language">official language</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Holy_See" title="Holy See">Holy See</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Rite" title="Roman Rite">Roman Rite</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">Catholic Church</a> at <a href="/wiki/Vatican_City" title="Vatican City">Vatican City</a>.
</p><p>Latin has also <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Latin_influence_on_English" title="Latin influence on English">greatly influenced</a> the English language and historically contributed <a href="/wiki/List_of_Latin_words_with_English_derivatives" title="List of Latin words with English derivatives">many words</a> to the English <a href="/wiki/Lexicon" title="Lexicon">lexicon</a> after the <a href="/wiki/Christianity_in_Anglo-Saxon_England" title="Christianity in Anglo-Saxon England">Christianization of Anglo-Saxons</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Norman_conquest" title="Norman conquest">Norman conquest</a>. In particular, Latin (and <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek" title="Ancient Greek">Ancient Greek</a>) <a href="/wiki/Root_(linguistics)" title="Root (linguistics)">roots</a> are still used in English descriptions of theology, <a href="/wiki/List_of_Latin_and_Greek_words_commonly_used_in_systematic_names" title="List of Latin and Greek words commonly used in systematic names">science disciplines</a> (especially <a href="/wiki/Anatomy" title="Anatomy">anatomy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Taxonomy_(biology)" title="Taxonomy (biology)">taxonomy</a>), <a href="/wiki/List_of_medical_roots,_suffixes_and_prefixes" title="List of medical roots, suffixes and prefixes">medicine</a>, and <a href="/wiki/List_of_Latin_legal_terms" title="List of Latin legal terms">law</a>.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Latin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/History_of_Latin" title="History of Latin">History of Latin</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Linguistic_Landscape_of_Central_Italy.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="990" data-file-width="2232" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Linguistic_Landscape_of_Central_Italy.png/330px-Linguistic_Landscape_of_Central_Italy.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Linguistic_Landscape_of_Central_Italy.png/495px-Linguistic_Landscape_of_Central_Italy.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Linguistic_Landscape_of_Central_Italy.png/660px-Linguistic_Landscape_of_Central_Italy.png 2x" width="330"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Linguistic_Landscape_of_Central_Italy.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The linguistic landscape of Central Italy at the beginning of Roman expansion</div></div></div>
<p>A number of historical phases of the language have been recognized, each distinguished by subtle differences in vocabulary, usage, spelling, <a href="/wiki/Morphology_(linguistics)" title="Morphology (linguistics)">morphology</a>, and syntax. There are no hard and fast rules of classification; different scholars emphasize different features. As a result, the list has variants, as well as alternative names.
</p><p>In addition to the historical phases, <a href="/wiki/Ecclesiastical_Latin" title="Ecclesiastical Latin">Ecclesiastical Latin</a> refers to the styles used by the writers of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_Catholic_Church" title="Roman Catholic Church">Roman Catholic Church</a> from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Late_Antiquity" title="Late Antiquity">Late Antiquity</a> onward, as well as by Protestant scholars.
</p><p>After the <a href="/wiki/Western_Roman_Empire" title="Western Roman Empire">Western Roman Empire</a> fell in 476 and Germanic kingdoms took its place, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Germanic_people" title="Germanic people">Germanic people</a> adopted Latin as a language more suitable for legal and other, more formal uses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup>
</p>
<div style="clear:both;"></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Old_Latin">Old Latin</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Latin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Old Latin">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Old_Latin" title="Old Latin">Old Latin</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Lapis-niger.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="403" data-file-width="289" decoding="async" height="307" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Lapis-niger.jpg/220px-Lapis-niger.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/f/fb/Lapis-niger.jpg 1.5x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Lapis-niger.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a href="/wiki/Lapis_Niger" title="Lapis Niger">Lapis Niger</a>, probably the oldest extant Latin inscription, from Rome, c. 600 BC during the semi-legendary <a href="/wiki/Roman_Kingdom" title="Roman Kingdom">Roman Kingdom</a></div></div></div>
<p>The earliest known form of Latin is Old Latin, which was spoken from the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Kingdom" title="Roman Kingdom">Roman Kingdom</a> to the later part of the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Republic" title="Roman Republic">Roman Republic</a> period. It is attested both in inscriptions and in some of the earliest extant Latin literary works, such as the comedies of <a href="/wiki/Plautus" title="Plautus">Plautus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Terence" title="Terence">Terence</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Latin_alphabet" title="Latin alphabet">Latin alphabet</a> was devised from the <a href="/wiki/Etruscan_alphabet" title="Etruscan alphabet">Etruscan alphabet</a>. The writing later changed from what was initially either a <a href="/wiki/Right-to-left_script" title="Right-to-left script">right-to-left</a> or a <a href="/wiki/Boustrophedon" title="Boustrophedon">boustrophedon</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> script to what ultimately became a strictly left-to-right script.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Classical_Latin">Classical Latin</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Latin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Classical Latin">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Classical_Latin" title="Classical Latin">Classical Latin</a></div>
<p>During the late republic and into the first years of the empire, a new <a href="/wiki/Classical_Latin" title="Classical Latin">Classical Latin</a> arose, a conscious creation of the orators, poets, historians and other <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Literate" title="Literate">literate</a> men, who wrote the great works of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Classical_literature" title="Classical literature">classical literature</a>, which were taught in <a href="/wiki/Grammar" title="Grammar">grammar</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rhetoric" title="Rhetoric">rhetoric</a> schools. Today's instructional grammars trace their roots to such <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_school" title="Roman school">schools</a>, which served as a sort of informal language academy dedicated to maintaining and perpetuating educated speech.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Vulgar_Latin">Vulgar Latin</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Latin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Vulgar Latin">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Vulgar_Latin" title="Vulgar Latin">Vulgar Latin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Late_Latin" title="Late Latin">Late Latin</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Romance_languages" title="Romance languages">Romance languages</a></div>
<p>Philological analysis of Archaic Latin works, such as those of <a href="/wiki/Plautus" title="Plautus">Plautus</a>, which contain fragments of everyday speech, indicates that a spoken language, Vulgar Latin (termed <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">sermo vulgi</i></span>, "the speech of the masses", by <a href="/wiki/Cicero" title="Cicero">Cicero</a>), existed concurrently with literate Classical Latin. The informal language was rarely written, so philologists have been left with only individual words and phrases cited by classical authors and those found as graffiti.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup>
As it was free to develop on its own, there is no reason to suppose that the speech was uniform either diachronically or geographically. On the contrary, Romanised European populations developed their own dialects of the language, which eventually led to the differentiation of <a href="/wiki/Romance_languages" title="Romance languages">Romance languages</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup>
</p><p>The Romance languages descend from Vulgar Latin and were originally the popular and informal dialects spoken by various layers of the Latin-speaking population. These dialects were distinct from the classical form of the language spoken by the Roman upper classes, the form in which Romans generally wrote. 
</p><p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Decline_of_the_Roman_Empire" title="Decline of the Roman Empire">decline of the Roman Empire</a> meant a deterioration in educational standards that brought about Late Latin, a postclassical stage of the language seen in Christian writings of the time. It was more in line with everyday speech, not only because of a decline in education but also because of a desire to spread the word to the masses.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2017)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>Currently, the five most widely spoken <a href="/wiki/Romance_languages" title="Romance languages">Romance languages</a> by number of native speakers are Spanish, <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_language" title="Portuguese language">Portuguese</a>, French, Italian and <a href="/wiki/Romanian_language" title="Romanian language">Romanian</a>. Despite dialectal variation, which is found in any widespread language, the languages of Spain, France, Portugal, and Italy have retained a remarkable unity in phonological forms and developments, bolstered by the stabilising influence of their common <a href="/wiki/Christians" title="Christians">Christian</a> (Roman Catholic) culture. It was not until the <a href="/wiki/Umayyad_conquest_of_Hispania" title="Umayyad conquest of Hispania">Muslim conquest of Spain</a> in 711, cutting off communications between the major Romance regions, that the languages began to diverge seriously.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> The Vulgar Latin dialect that would later become <a href="/wiki/Romanian_language" title="Romanian language">Romanian</a> diverged somewhat more from the other varieties, as it was largely separated from the unifying influences in the western part of the Empire.
</p><p>One key marker of whether a given Romance feature was found in Vulgar Latin is to compare it with its parallel in Classical Latin. If it was not preferred in Classical Latin, then it most likely came from the undocumented contemporaneous Vulgar Latin. For example, the Romance for "horse" (Italian <span title="Italian-language text"><i lang="it">cavallo</i></span>, French <span title="French-language text"><i lang="fr">cheval</i></span>, Spanish <span title="Spanish-language text"><i lang="es">caballo</i></span>, Portuguese <span title="Portuguese-language text"><i lang="pt">cavalo</i></span> and Romanian <span title="Romanian-language text"><i lang="ro">cal</i></span>) came from Latin <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">caballus</i></span>. However, Classical Latin used <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">equus</i></span>. Therefore, <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">caballus</i></span> was most likely the spoken form.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup>
</p><p>Vulgar Latin began to diverge into distinct languages by the 9th century at the latest, when the earliest extant Romance writings begin to appear. They were, throughout the period, confined to everyday speech, as Medieval Latin was used for writing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Medieval_Latin">Medieval Latin</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Latin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Medieval Latin">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Medieval_Latin" title="Medieval Latin">Medieval Latin</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Calligraphy.malmesbury.bible.arp.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1011" data-file-width="1500" decoding="async" height="169" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Calligraphy.malmesbury.bible.arp.jpg/250px-Calligraphy.malmesbury.bible.arp.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Calligraphy.malmesbury.bible.arp.jpg/375px-Calligraphy.malmesbury.bible.arp.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Calligraphy.malmesbury.bible.arp.jpg/500px-Calligraphy.malmesbury.bible.arp.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Calligraphy.malmesbury.bible.arp.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Latin Malmesbury Bible from 1407</div></div></div>
<p>Medieval Latin is the written Latin in use during that portion of the postclassical period when no corresponding Latin vernacular existed. The spoken language had developed into the various incipient Romance languages; however, in the educated and official world, Latin continued without its natural spoken base. Moreover, this Latin spread into lands that had never spoken Latin, such as the Germanic and Slavic nations. It became useful for international communication between the member states of the <a href="/wiki/Holy_Roman_Empire" title="Holy Roman Empire">Holy Roman Empire</a> and its allies.
</p><p>Without the institutions of the Roman Empire that had supported its uniformity, medieval Latin lost its linguistic cohesion: for example, in classical Latin <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">sum</i></span> and <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">eram</i></span> are used as auxiliary verbs in the perfect and pluperfect passive, which are compound tenses. Medieval Latin might use <span title="Medieval Latin-language text"><i lang="la">fui</i></span> and <span title="Medieval Latin-language text"><i lang="la">fueram</i></span> instead.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thorley13-15_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-thorley13-15-16">[16]</a></sup> Furthermore, the meanings of many words have been changed and new vocabularies have been introduced from the vernacular. Identifiable individual styles of classically incorrect Latin prevail.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-thorley13-15_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-thorley13-15-16">[16]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Renaissance_Latin">Renaissance Latin</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Latin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Renaissance Latin">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Renaissance_Latin" title="Renaissance Latin">Renaissance Latin</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Incunabula_distribution_by_language.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="950" data-file-width="1394" decoding="async" height="150" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Incunabula_distribution_by_language.png/220px-Incunabula_distribution_by_language.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Incunabula_distribution_by_language.png/330px-Incunabula_distribution_by_language.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Incunabula_distribution_by_language.png/440px-Incunabula_distribution_by_language.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Incunabula_distribution_by_language.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Most 15th-century printed books (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Incunabula" title="Incunabula">incunabula</a>) were in Latin, with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vernacular_language" title="Vernacular language">vernacular languages</a> playing only a secondary role.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ISTC_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-ISTC-17">[17]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Renaissance" title="Renaissance">Renaissance</a> briefly reinforced the position of Latin as a spoken language by its adoption by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Renaissance_Humanism" title="Renaissance Humanism">Renaissance Humanists</a>. Often led by members of the clergy, they were shocked by the accelerated dismantling of the vestiges of the classical world and the rapid loss of its literature. They strove to preserve what they could and restore Latin to what it had been and introduced the practice of producing revised editions of the literary works that remained by comparing surviving manuscripts. By no later than the 15th century they had replaced Medieval Latin with versions supported by the scholars of the rising universities, who attempted, by scholarship, to discover what the classical language had been.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_14-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-14">[14]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="New_Latin">New Latin</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Latin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: New Latin">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/New_Latin" title="New Latin">New Latin</a></div>
<p>During the Early Modern Age, Latin still was the most important language of culture in Europe. Therefore, until the end of the 17th century, the majority of books and almost all diplomatic documents were written in Latin.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Afterwards, most diplomatic documents were written in French (a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Romance_language" title="Romance language">Romance language</a>) and later native or other languages.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Contemporary_Latin">Contemporary Latin</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Latin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Contemporary Latin">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Contemporary_Latin" title="Contemporary Latin">Contemporary Latin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Ecclesiastical_Latin" title="Ecclesiastical Latin">Ecclesiastical Latin</a></div>
<p>Despite having no native speakers, Latin is still used for a variety of purposes in the contemporary world.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Religious_use">Religious use</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Latin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Religious use">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Wallsend_platfom_2_02.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1600" data-file-width="1200" decoding="async" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Wallsend_platfom_2_02.jpg/220px-Wallsend_platfom_2_02.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Wallsend_platfom_2_02.jpg/330px-Wallsend_platfom_2_02.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Wallsend_platfom_2_02.jpg/440px-Wallsend_platfom_2_02.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Wallsend_platfom_2_02.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The signs at <a href="/wiki/Wallsend_Metro_station" title="Wallsend Metro station">Wallsend Metro station</a> are in English and Latin, as a tribute to <a href="/wiki/Wallsend" title="Wallsend">Wallsend</a>'s role as one of the outposts of the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman Empire</a>, as the eastern end of <a href="/wiki/Hadrian%27s_Wall" title="Hadrian's Wall">Hadrian's Wall</a> (hence the name) at <a href="/wiki/Segedunum" title="Segedunum">Segedunum</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>The largest organisation that retains Latin in official and quasi-official contexts is the <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">Catholic Church</a>. The Catholic Church required that Mass be carried out in Latin until the Second Vatican Council of 1962–1965, which permitted the use of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vernacular_language" title="Vernacular language">vernacular</a>. Latin remains the language of the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Rite" title="Roman Rite">Roman Rite</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Tridentine_Mass" title="Tridentine Mass">Tridentine Mass</a> (also known as the Extraordinary Form or Traditional Latin Mass) is celebrated in Latin. Although the <a href="/wiki/Mass_of_Paul_VI" title="Mass of Paul VI">Mass of Paul VI</a> (also known as the Ordinary Form or the Novus Ordo) is usually celebrated in the local vernacular language, it can be and often is said in Latin, in part or in whole, especially at multilingual gatherings. It is the official language of the <a href="/wiki/Holy_See" title="Holy See">Holy See</a>, the primary language of its <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Public_journal" title="Public journal">public journal</a>, the <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la"><a href="/wiki/Acta_Apostolicae_Sedis" title="Acta Apostolicae Sedis">Acta Apostolicae Sedis</a></i></span>, and the working language of the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Rota" title="Roman Rota">Roman Rota</a>. <a href="/wiki/Vatican_City" title="Vatican City">Vatican City</a> is also home to the world's only <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Automatic_teller_machine" title="Automatic teller machine">automatic teller machine</a> that gives instructions in Latin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup> In the <a href="/wiki/Pontifical_university" title="Pontifical university">pontifical universities</a> postgraduate courses of <a href="/wiki/Canon_law" title="Canon law">Canon law</a> are taught in Latin, and papers are written in the same language.
</p><p>In the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anglican_Church" title="Anglican Church">Anglican Church</a>, after the publication of the <i><a href="/wiki/Book_of_Common_Prayer" title="Book of Common Prayer">Book of Common Prayer</a></i> of 1559, a Latin edition was published in 1560 for use in universities such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oxford_University" title="Oxford University">Oxford</a> and the leading "public schools" (English private academies), where the liturgy was still permitted to be conducted in Latin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> There have been several Latin translations since, including a Latin edition of the 1979 USA Anglican Book of Common Prayer.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Former_logo_of_the_European_Council_and_Council_of_the_European_Union_(2009).svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="224" data-file-width="380" decoding="async" height="130" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Former_logo_of_the_European_Council_and_Council_of_the_European_Union_%282009%29.svg/220px-Former_logo_of_the_European_Council_and_Council_of_the_European_Union_%282009%29.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Former_logo_of_the_European_Council_and_Council_of_the_European_Union_%282009%29.svg/330px-Former_logo_of_the_European_Council_and_Council_of_the_European_Union_%282009%29.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/87/Former_logo_of_the_European_Council_and_Council_of_the_European_Union_%282009%29.svg/440px-Former_logo_of_the_European_Council_and_Council_of_the_European_Union_%282009%29.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Former_logo_of_the_European_Council_and_Council_of_the_European_Union_(2009).svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The polyglot <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a> has adopted Latin names in the logos of some of its institutions for the sake of linguistic compromise, an "ecumenical nationalism" common to most of the continent and as a sign of the continent's heritage (such as the <a href="/wiki/Council_of_the_European_Union" title="Council of the European Union">EU Council</a>: <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">Consilium</i></span>).</div></div></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Use_of_Latin_for_mottos">Use of Latin for mottos</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Latin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Use of Latin for mottos">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>In the Philippines and in the Western world, many organizations, governments and schools use Latin for their mottos due to its association with formality, tradition, and the roots of <a href="/wiki/Western_culture" title="Western culture">Western culture</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup>
</p><p>Canada's motto <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">A mari usque ad mare</i></span> ("from sea to sea") and most <a href="/wiki/List_of_Canadian_provincial_and_territorial_symbols" title="List of Canadian provincial and territorial symbols">provincial mottos</a> are also in Latin. The <a href="/wiki/Victoria_Cross_(Canada)" title="Victoria Cross (Canada)">Canadian Victoria Cross</a> is modelled after the British <a href="/wiki/Victoria_Cross" title="Victoria Cross">Victoria Cross</a> which has the inscription "For Valour". Because Canada is officially bilingual, the Canadian medal has replaced the English inscription with the Latin <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">Pro Valore</i></span>.
</p><p>Spain's motto <i><a href="/wiki/Plus_ultra" title="Plus ultra">Plus ultra</a></i>, meaning "even further", or figuratively "Further!", is also Latin in origin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> It is taken from the personal motto of <a href="/wiki/Charles_V,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Charles V, Holy Roman Emperor">Charles V</a>, Holy Roman Emperor and King of Spain (as Charles I), and is a reversal of the original phrase <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">Non terrae plus ultra</i></span> ("No land further beyond", "No further!"). According to <a href="/wiki/Legend" title="Legend">legend</a>, this inscribed as a warning on the <a href="/wiki/Pillars_of_Hercules" title="Pillars of Hercules">Pillars of Hercules</a>, the rocks on both sides of the <a href="/wiki/Strait_of_Gibraltar" title="Strait of Gibraltar">Strait of Gibraltar</a> and the western end of the known, Mediterranean world. Charles adopted the motto following the discovery of the New World by Columbus, and it also has metaphorical suggestions of taking risks and striving for excellence.
</p><p>Several <a href="/wiki/List_of_U.S._state_and_territory_mottos" title="List of U.S. state and territory mottos">states of the United States have Latin mottos</a>, such as:
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Arizona" title="Arizona">Arizona</a>'s <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">Ditat dues</i></span> ("God enriches");</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Connecticut" title="Connecticut">Connecticut</a>'s <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">Qui transtulit sustinet</i></span> ("He who transplanted sustains");</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Kansas" title="Kansas">Kansas</a>'s <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la"><a href="/wiki/Per_aspera_ad_astra" title="Per aspera ad astra">Ad astra per aspera</a></i></span> ("Through hardships, to the stars");</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Colorado" title="Colorado">Colorado</a>'s <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">Nil sine numine</i></span> ("Nothing without providence");</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Michigan" title="Michigan">Michigan</a>'s <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">Si quaeris peninsulam amoenam, circumspice</i></span> ("If you seek a pleasant peninsula, look about you"), is based on that of Sir <a href="/wiki/Christopher_Wren" title="Christopher Wren">Christopher Wren</a>, in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/St._Paul%27s_Cathedral" title="St. Paul's Cathedral">St. Paul's Cathedral</a>;</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Missouri" title="Missouri">Missouri</a>'s <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la"><a href="/wiki/Salus_populi_suprema_lex_esto" title="Salus populi suprema lex esto">Salus populi suprema lex esto</a></i></span> ("The health of the people should be the highest law");</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/New_York_(state)" title="New York (state)">New York (state)</a>'s <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la"><a href="/wiki/Coat_of_arms_of_New_York" title="Coat of arms of New York">Excelsior</a></i></span> ("Ever upward");</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/North_Carolina" title="North Carolina">North Carolina</a>'s <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Esse_Quam_Videri" title="Esse Quam Videri">Esse Quam Videri</a></i></span> ("To be rather than to seem");</li>
<li>South Carolina's <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la"><a href="/wiki/Dum_spiro_spero" title="Dum spiro spero">Dum spiro spero</a></i></span> ("While [still] breathing, I hope");</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Virginia" title="Virginia">Virginia</a>'s <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la"><a href="/wiki/Sic_semper_tyrannis" title="Sic semper tyrannis">Sic semper tyrannis</a></i></span> ("Thus always to <a href="/wiki/Tyrant" title="Tyrant">tyrants</a>"); and</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/West_Virginia" title="West Virginia">West Virginia</a>'s <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Montani_Semper_Liberi" title="Montani Semper Liberi">Montani Semper Liberi</a></i></span> ("Mountaineers [are] always free").</li></ul>
<p>Many military organizations today have Latin mottos, such as:
</p>
<ul><li><span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Semper_Paratus" title="Semper Paratus">Semper Paratus</a></i></span> ("always ready"), the motto of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Coast_Guard" title="United States Coast Guard">United States Coast Guard</a>;</li>
<li><span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la"><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Semper_Fidelis" title="Semper Fidelis">Semper Fidelis</a></i></span> ("always faithful"), the motto of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Marine_Corps" title="United States Marine Corps">United States Marine Corps</a>;</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Semper_supra" title="Semper supra">Semper supra</a> ("always above"), the motto of the <a href="/wiki/United_States_Space_Force" title="United States Space Force">United States Space Force</a>;</li>
<li><span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la"><a href="/wiki/Per_ardua_ad_astra" title="Per ardua ad astra">Per ardua ad astra</a></i></span> ("Through adversity/struggle to the stars"), the motto of the <a href="/wiki/Royal_Air_Force" title="Royal Air Force">Royal Air Force</a> (RAF); and</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/List_of_military_unit_mottoes_by_country" title="List of military unit mottoes by country">Vigilamus pro te</a> ("We stand on guard for thee"), the motto of the <a href="/wiki/Canadian_Armed_Forces" title="Canadian Armed Forces">Canadian Armed Forces</a>.</li></ul>
<p>A law governing body in the Philippines have a Latin motto, such as:
</p>
<ul><li><span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">Justitiae Pax Opus</i></span> ("Justice, peace, work"), the motto of the <a href="/wiki/Department_of_Justice_(Philippines)" title="Department of Justice (Philippines)">Department of Justice (Philippines)</a>;</li></ul>
<p>Some colleges and universities have adopted Latin mottos, for example <a href="/wiki/Harvard_University" title="Harvard University">Harvard University</a>'s motto is <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la"><a href="/wiki/Veritas" title="Veritas">Veritas</a></i></span> ("truth"). Veritas was the goddess of truth, a daughter of Saturn, and the mother of Virtue.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_modern_uses">Other modern uses</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Latin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Other modern uses">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Switzerland has adopted the country's Latin short name <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">Helvetia</i></span> on coins and stamps, since there is no room to use all of the nation's <a href="/wiki/Languages_of_Switzerland" title="Languages of Switzerland">four official languages</a>. For a similar reason, it adopted the international vehicle and internet code <i>CH</i>, which stands for <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">Confœderatio Helvetica</i></span>, the country's full Latin name.
</p><p>Some films of ancient settings, such as <i><a href="/wiki/Sebastiane" title="Sebastiane">Sebastiane</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/The_Passion_of_the_Christ" title="The Passion of the Christ">The Passion of the Christ</a></i>, have been made with dialogue in Latin for the sake of realism. Occasionally, Latin dialogue is used because of its association with religion or philosophy, in such film/television series as <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/The_Exorcist_(film)" title="The Exorcist (film)">The Exorcist</a></i> and <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lost_(2004_TV_series)" title="Lost (2004 TV series)">Lost</a></i> ("<a href="/wiki/Jughead_(Lost)" title="Jughead (Lost)">Jughead</a>"). Subtitles are usually shown for the benefit of those who do not understand Latin. There are also <a href="/wiki/List_of_songs_with_Latin_lyrics" title="List of songs with Latin lyrics">songs written with Latin lyrics</a>. The libretto for the opera-oratorio <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la"><a href="/wiki/Oedipus_rex_(opera)" title="Oedipus rex (opera)">Oedipus rex</a></i></span> by <a href="/wiki/Igor_Stravinsky" title="Igor Stravinsky">Igor Stravinsky</a> is in Latin.
</p><p>The continued instruction of Latin is often seen as a highly valuable component of a liberal arts education. Latin is taught at many high schools, especially in Europe and the Americas. It is most common in British <a href="/wiki/Public_school_(United_Kingdom)" title="Public school (United Kingdom)">public schools</a> and grammar schools, the Italian <span title="Italian-language text"><i lang="it"><a href="/wiki/Liceo_classico" title="Liceo classico">liceo classico</a></i></span> and <span title="Italian-language text"><i lang="it"><a href="/wiki/Liceo_scientifico" title="Liceo scientifico">liceo scientifico</a></i></span>, the German <span title="German-language text"><i lang="de">Humanistisches <a href="/wiki/Gymnasium_(Germany)" title="Gymnasium (Germany)">Gymnasium</a></i></span> and the Dutch <span title="Dutch-language text"><i lang="nl"><a href="/wiki/Gymnasium_(school)" title="Gymnasium (school)">gymnasium</a></i></span>.
</p>

codecs="vp8, vorbis"'/></video> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:QDP_Ep_84_-_De_Ludo_%22Mysterium%22.webm" title="Enlarge"></a></div>QDP Ep 84 – De Ludo "Mysterium": A Latin language podcast from the USA</div></div></div><p> Occasionally, some media outlets, targeting enthusiasts, broadcast in Latin. Notable examples include <a href="/wiki/Radio_Bremen" title="Radio Bremen">Radio Bremen</a> in Germany, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/YLE" title="YLE">YLE</a> radio in Finland (the <a href="/wiki/Nuntii_Latini" title="Nuntii Latini">Nuntii Latini</a> broadcast from 1989 until it was shut down in June 2019),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-RTE2019-06-24a_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-RTE2019-06-24a-24">[24]</a></sup> and Vatican Radio &amp; Television, all of which broadcast news segments and other material in Latin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup>
</p><p>A variety of organisations, as well as informal Latin 'circuli' ('circles'), have been founded in more recent times to support the use of spoken Latin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> Moreover, a number of university classics departments have begun incorporating communicative pedagogies in their Latin courses. These include the University of Kentucky, the University of Oxford and also Princeton University.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup>
</p><p>There are many websites and forums maintained in Latin by enthusiasts. The <a href="/wiki/Latin_Wikipedia" title="Latin Wikipedia">Latin Wikipedia</a> has more than 130,000 articles.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Urdaneta,_Pangasinan" title="Urdaneta, Pangasinan">Urdaneta City</a>'s motto <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">Deo servire populo sufficere</i></span> ("It is enough for the people to serve God") the Latin motto can be read in the old seal of this Philippine city.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Legacy">Legacy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Latin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Legacy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Italian, French, <a href="/wiki/Portuguese_language" title="Portuguese language">Portuguese</a>, Spanish, <a href="/wiki/Romanian_language" title="Romanian language">Romanian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Catalan_language" title="Catalan language">Catalan</a>, <a href="/wiki/Romansh_language" title="Romansh language">Romansh</a> and other <a href="/wiki/Romance_languages" title="Romance languages">Romance languages</a> are direct descendants of Latin. There are also many Latin <a href="/wiki/Loanword" title="Loanword">borrowings</a> in English and <a href="/wiki/Albanian_language" title="Albanian language">Albanian</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sawicka_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sawicka-31">[31]</a></sup> as well as a few in German, <a href="/wiki/Dutch_language" title="Dutch language">Dutch</a>, <a href="/wiki/Norwegian_language" title="Norwegian language">Norwegian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Danish_language" title="Danish language">Danish</a> and <a href="/wiki/Swedish_language" title="Swedish language">Swedish</a>. Latin is still spoken in Vatican City, a city-state situated in Rome that is the seat of the <a href="/wiki/Catholic_Church" title="Catholic Church">Catholic Church</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Inscriptions">Inscriptions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Latin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Inscriptions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Some inscriptions have been published in an internationally agreed, monumental, multivolume series, the <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la"><a href="/wiki/Corpus_Inscriptionum_Latinarum" title="Corpus Inscriptionum Latinarum">Corpus Inscriptionum Latinarum</a></i></span> (CIL). Authors and publishers vary, but the format is about the same: volumes detailing inscriptions with a critical apparatus stating the <a href="/wiki/Provenance" title="Provenance">provenance</a> and relevant information. The reading and interpretation of these inscriptions is the subject matter of the field of <a href="/wiki/Epigraphy" title="Epigraphy">epigraphy</a>. About 270,000 inscriptions are known.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Literature">Literature</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Latin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Literature">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Commentarii_de_Bello_Gallico.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="509" data-file-width="640" decoding="async" height="239" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Commentarii_de_Bello_Gallico.jpg/300px-Commentarii_de_Bello_Gallico.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Commentarii_de_Bello_Gallico.jpg/450px-Commentarii_de_Bello_Gallico.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Commentarii_de_Bello_Gallico.jpg/600px-Commentarii_de_Bello_Gallico.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Commentarii_de_Bello_Gallico.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Julius_Caesar" title="Julius Caesar">Julius Caesar</a>'s <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la"><a href="/wiki/Commentarii_de_Bello_Gallico" title="Commentarii de Bello Gallico">Commentarii de Bello Gallico</a></i></span> is one of the most famous classical Latin texts of the Golden Age of Latin. The unvarnished, journalistic style of this <a href="/wiki/Patrician_(ancient_Rome)" title="Patrician (ancient Rome)">patrician</a> general has long been taught as a model of the urbane Latin officially spoken and written in the <a href="/wiki/Floruit" title="Floruit">floruit</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Republic" title="Roman Republic">Roman Republic</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>The works of several hundred ancient authors who wrote in Latin have survived in whole or in part, in substantial works or in fragments to be analyzed in <a href="/wiki/Philology" title="Philology">philology</a>. They are in part the subject matter of the field of <a href="/wiki/Classics" title="Classics">classics</a>. Their works were published in manuscript form before the invention of printing and are now published in carefully annotated printed editions, such as the <a href="/wiki/Loeb_Classical_Library" title="Loeb Classical Library">Loeb Classical Library</a>, published by <a href="/wiki/Harvard_University_Press" title="Harvard University Press">Harvard University Press</a>, or the <a href="/wiki/Oxford_Classical_Texts" title="Oxford Classical Texts">Oxford Classical Texts</a>, published by <a href="/wiki/Oxford_University_Press" title="Oxford University Press">Oxford University Press</a>.
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Latin_translations_of_modern_literature" title="Latin translations of modern literature">Latin translations of modern literature</a> such as: <i><a href="/wiki/The_Hobbit" title="The Hobbit">The Hobbit</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Treasure_Island" title="Treasure Island">Treasure Island</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Robinson_Crusoe" title="Robinson Crusoe">Robinson Crusoe</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Paddington_Bear" title="Paddington Bear">Paddington Bear</a></i>, <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Winnie_the_Pooh" title="Winnie the Pooh">Winnie the Pooh</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Adventures_of_Tintin" title="The Adventures of Tintin">The Adventures of Tintin</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Asterix" title="Asterix">Asterix</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Harry_Potter" title="Harry Potter">Harry Potter</a></i>, <span title="French-language text"><i lang="fr"><a href="/wiki/The_Little_Prince" title="The Little Prince">Le Petit Prince</a></i></span>, <i><a href="/wiki/Max_and_Moritz" title="Max and Moritz">Max and Moritz</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/How_the_Grinch_Stole_Christmas!" title="How the Grinch Stole Christmas!">How the Grinch Stole Christmas!</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/The_Cat_in_the_Hat" title="The Cat in the Hat">The Cat in the Hat</a></i>, and a book of fairy tales, "<span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">fabulae mirabiles</i></span>", are intended to garner popular interest in the language. Additional resources include phrasebooks and resources for rendering everyday phrases and concepts into Latin, such as <a href="/wiki/Meissner%27s_Latin_Phrasebook" title="Meissner's Latin Phrasebook">Meissner's Latin Phrasebook</a>.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Influence_on_present-day_languages">Influence on present-day languages</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Latin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Influence on present-day languages">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Latin_influence_in_English" title="Latin influence in English">Latin influence in English</a> has been significant at all stages of its insular development. In the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a>, borrowing from Latin occurred from ecclesiastical usage established by Saint <a href="/wiki/Augustine_of_Canterbury" title="Augustine of Canterbury">Augustine of Canterbury</a> in the 6th century or indirectly after the <a href="/wiki/Norman_Conquest" title="Norman Conquest">Norman Conquest</a>, through the <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Norman_language" title="Anglo-Norman language">Anglo-Norman language</a>. From the 16th to the 18th centuries, English writers cobbled together huge numbers of new words from Latin and Greek words, dubbed "<a href="/wiki/Inkhorn_term" title="Inkhorn term">inkhorn terms</a>", as if they had spilled from a pot of ink. Many of these words were used once by the author and then forgotten, but some useful ones survived, such as 'imbibe' and 'extrapolate'. Many of the most common <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polysyllabic" title="Polysyllabic">polysyllabic</a> English words are of Latin origin through the medium of <a href="/wiki/Old_French" title="Old French">Old French</a>. Romance words make respectively 59%, 20% and 14% of English, German and Dutch vocabularies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Walter_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-Walter-34">[34]</a></sup> Those figures can rise dramatically when only non-compound and non-derived words are included.
</p><p>The influence of Roman governance and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_technology" title="Roman technology">Roman technology</a> on the less-developed nations under Roman dominion led to the adoption of Latin phraseology in some specialized areas, such as science, technology, medicine, and law. For example, <a href="/wiki/Linnaean_taxonomy" title="Linnaean taxonomy">the Linnaean system</a> of plant and animal classification was heavily influenced by <i><a href="/wiki/Natural_History_(Pliny)" title="Natural History (Pliny)">Historia Naturalis</a></i>, an encyclopedia of people, places, plants, animals, and things published by <a href="/wiki/Pliny_the_Elder" title="Pliny the Elder">Pliny the Elder</a>. Roman medicine, recorded in the works of such physicians as <a href="/wiki/Galen" title="Galen">Galen</a>, established that today's <a href="/wiki/Medical_terminology" title="Medical terminology">medical terminology</a> would be primarily derived from Latin and Greek words, the Greek being filtered through the Latin. Roman engineering had the same effect on <a href="/wiki/Scientific_terminology" title="Scientific terminology">scientific terminology</a> as a whole. Latin law principles have survived partly in a long <a href="/wiki/List_of_Latin_legal_terms" title="List of Latin legal terms">list of Latin legal terms</a>.
</p><p>A few <a href="/wiki/International_auxiliary_language" title="International auxiliary language">international auxiliary languages</a> have been heavily influenced by Latin. <a href="/wiki/Interlingua" title="Interlingua">Interlingua</a> is sometimes considered a simplified, modern version of the language.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Accuracy_dispute#Disputed_statement" title="Wikipedia:Accuracy dispute"><span title="The material near this tag is possibly inaccurate or nonfactual. (January 2017)">dubious</span></a> <span class="metadata"> – <a href="/wiki/Talk:Latin#Dubious" title="Talk:Latin">discuss</a></span></i>]</sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Latino_sine_Flexione" title="Latino sine Flexione">Latino sine Flexione</a>, popular in the early 20th century, is Latin with its inflections stripped away, among other grammatical changes.
</p><p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Logudorese" title="Logudorese">Logudorese</a> dialect of the <a href="/wiki/Sardinian_language" title="Sardinian language">Sardinian language</a> is the closest contemporary language to Latin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Education">Education</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Latin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Education">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Latin_dictionary.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1120" data-file-width="1408" decoding="async" height="175" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Latin_dictionary.jpg/220px-Latin_dictionary.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Latin_dictionary.jpg/330px-Latin_dictionary.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6e/Latin_dictionary.jpg/440px-Latin_dictionary.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Latin_dictionary.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A multivolume Latin dictionary in the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Graz_Library" title="University of Graz Library">University of Graz Library</a> in Austria.</div></div></div>
<p>Throughout European history, an education in the classics was considered crucial for those who wished to join literate circles. This also was true in the United States where many of the nation's Founders obtained a classically-based education in grammar schools or from tutors.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> Admission to Harvard in the Colonial era required that the applicant "Can readily make and speak or write true Latin prose and has skill in making verse . . ."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-37"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup> Latin Study and the classics were emphasized in American secondary schools and colleges well into the Antebellum era.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Instruction_in_Latin" title="Instruction in Latin">Instruction in Latin</a> is an essential aspect. In today's world, a large number of Latin students in the US learn from <i>Wheelock's Latin: The Classic Introductory Latin Course, Based on Ancient Authors</i>. This book, first published in 1956,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup> was written by <a href="/wiki/Frederic_M._Wheelock" title="Frederic M. Wheelock">Frederic M. Wheelock</a>, who received a PhD from Harvard University. <i>Wheelock's Latin</i> has become the standard text for many American introductory Latin courses.
</p><p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Living_Latin" title="Living Latin">Living Latin</a> movement attempts to teach Latin in the same way that living languages are taught, as a means of both spoken and written communication. It is available in Vatican City and at some institutions in the US, such as the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Kentucky" title="University of Kentucky">University of Kentucky</a> and <a href="/wiki/Iowa_State_University" title="Iowa State University">Iowa State University</a>. The British <a href="/wiki/Cambridge_University_Press" title="Cambridge University Press">Cambridge University Press</a> is a major supplier of Latin textbooks for all levels, such as the <a href="/wiki/Cambridge_Latin_Course" title="Cambridge Latin Course">Cambridge Latin Course</a> series. It has also published a subseries of children's texts in Latin by Bell &amp; Forte, which recounts the adventures of a mouse called <a href="/wiki/Minimus" title="Minimus">Minimus</a>.
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Latin_and_Ancient_Greek_Language_-_Culture_-_Linguistics_at_Duke_University_in_2014.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="5184" data-file-width="3456" decoding="async" height="300" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Latin_and_Ancient_Greek_Language_-_Culture_-_Linguistics_at_Duke_University_in_2014.jpg/200px-Latin_and_Ancient_Greek_Language_-_Culture_-_Linguistics_at_Duke_University_in_2014.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Latin_and_Ancient_Greek_Language_-_Culture_-_Linguistics_at_Duke_University_in_2014.jpg/300px-Latin_and_Ancient_Greek_Language_-_Culture_-_Linguistics_at_Duke_University_in_2014.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Latin_and_Ancient_Greek_Language_-_Culture_-_Linguistics_at_Duke_University_in_2014.jpg/400px-Latin_and_Ancient_Greek_Language_-_Culture_-_Linguistics_at_Duke_University_in_2014.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Latin_and_Ancient_Greek_Language_-_Culture_-_Linguistics_at_Duke_University_in_2014.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Latin and Ancient Greek at <a href="/wiki/Duke_University" title="Duke University">Duke University</a> in <a href="/wiki/Durham,_North_Carolina" title="Durham, North Carolina">Durham, North Carolina</a>, 2014.</div></div></div>
<p>In the United Kingdom, the <a href="/wiki/Classical_Association" title="Classical Association">Classical Association</a> encourages the study of antiquity through various means, such as publications and grants. The <a href="/wiki/University_of_Cambridge" title="University of Cambridge">University of Cambridge</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Open_University" title="Open University">Open University</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-41"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup> a number of prestigious independent schools, for example <a href="/wiki/Eton_College" title="Eton College">Eton</a>, <a href="/wiki/Harrow_School" title="Harrow School">Harrow</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Haberdashers%27_Aske%27s_Boys%27_School" title="Haberdashers' Aske's Boys' School">Haberdashers' Aske's Boys' School</a>, <a href="/wiki/Merchant_Taylors%27_School,_Northwood" title="Merchant Taylors' School, Northwood">Merchant Taylors' School</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Rugby_School" title="Rugby School">Rugby</a>, and The Latin Programme/Via Facilis,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> a London-based charity, run Latin courses. In the United States and in Canada, the <a href="/wiki/American_Classical_League" title="American Classical League">American Classical League</a> supports every effort to further the study of classics. Its subsidiaries include the <a href="/wiki/National_Junior_Classical_League" title="National Junior Classical League">National Junior Classical League</a> (with more than 50,000 members), which encourages high school students to pursue the study of Latin, and the <a href="/wiki/National_Senior_Classical_League" title="National Senior Classical League">National Senior Classical League</a>, which encourages students to continue their study of the classics into college. The league also sponsors the <a href="/wiki/National_Latin_Exam" title="National Latin Exam">National Latin Exam</a>. Classicist <a href="/wiki/Mary_Beard_(classicist)" title="Mary Beard (classicist)">Mary Beard</a> wrote in <i><a href="/wiki/The_Times_Literary_Supplement" title="The Times Literary Supplement">The Times Literary Supplement</a></i> in 2006 that the reason for learning Latin is because of what was written in it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-timesonline_train_the_brain_43-0"><a href="#cite_note-timesonline_train_the_brain-43">[43]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Official_status">Official status</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Latin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Official status">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Latin was or is the official language of European states:
</p>
<ul><li><span class="flagicon"><img alt="" class="thumbborder" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="1200" decoding="async" height="12" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Flag_of_Hungary.svg/23px-Flag_of_Hungary.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Flag_of_Hungary.svg/35px-Flag_of_Hungary.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c1/Flag_of_Hungary.svg/46px-Flag_of_Hungary.svg.png 2x" width="23"/> </span><a href="/wiki/Hungary" title="Hungary">Hungary</a> – Latin was an official language in the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Hungary" title="Kingdom of Hungary">Kingdom of Hungary</a> from the 11th century to the mid 19th century, when <a href="/wiki/Hungarian_language" title="Hungarian language">Hungarian</a> became the exclusive official language in 1844.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The best known Latin language poet of Croatian-Hungarian origin was <a href="/wiki/Janus_Pannonius" title="Janus Pannonius">Janus Pannonius</a>.</li>
<li><span class="flagicon"><img alt="" class="thumbborder" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="1200" decoding="async" height="12" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Flag_of_Croatia.svg/23px-Flag_of_Croatia.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Flag_of_Croatia.svg/35px-Flag_of_Croatia.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Flag_of_Croatia.svg/46px-Flag_of_Croatia.svg.png 2x" width="23"/> </span><a href="/wiki/Croatia" title="Croatia">Croatia</a> – Latin was the official language of <a href="/wiki/Croatian_Parliament" title="Croatian Parliament">Croatian Parliament</a> (Sabor) from the 13th to the 19th century (1847).<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (May 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The oldest preserved records of the parliamentary sessions (<span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">Congregatio Regni totius Sclavonie generalis</i></span>) – held in Zagreb (<span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">Zagabria</i></span>), Croatia – date from 19 April 1273. An extensive <a href="/wiki/Croatian_Latin_literature" title="Croatian Latin literature">Croatian Latin literature</a> exists. Latin is still used on Croatian coins on even years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup></li>
<li><span class="flagicon"><img alt="" class="thumbborder" data-file-height="800" data-file-width="1280" decoding="async" height="14" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/1/12/Flag_of_Poland.svg/23px-Flag_of_Poland.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/1/12/Flag_of_Poland.svg/35px-Flag_of_Poland.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/1/12/Flag_of_Poland.svg/46px-Flag_of_Poland.svg.png 2x" width="23"/> </span><a href="/wiki/Poland" title="Poland">Poland</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Poland" title="Kingdom of Poland">Kingdom of Poland</a> – officially recognised and widely used<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Friedrich_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-Friedrich-48">[48]</a></sup> between the 10th and 18th centuries, commonly used in foreign relations and popular as a second language among some of the <a href="/wiki/Szlachta" title="Szlachta">nobility</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Friedrich_48-1"><a href="#cite_note-Friedrich-48">[48]</a></sup></li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Phonology">Phonology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Latin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Phonology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Latin_phonology_and_orthography" title="Latin phonology and orthography">Latin phonology and orthography</a></div>
<p>The ancient pronunciation of Latin has been reconstructed; among the data used for reconstruction are explicit statements about pronunciation by ancient authors, misspellings, puns, ancient etymologies, the spelling of Latin loanwords in other languages, and the historical development of Romance languages.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Consonants">Consonants</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Latin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Consonants">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The consonant <a href="/wiki/Phoneme" title="Phoneme">phonemes</a> of Classical Latin are as follows:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup>
</p>

<p><span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/z/</span> was not native to Classical Latin. It appeared in Greek loanwords starting around the first century BC, when it was probably pronounced <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[z]</span> initially and <a href="/wiki/Gemination" title="Gemination">doubled</a> <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[zz]</span> between vowels, in contrast to <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek" title="Ancient Greek">Classical Greek</a> <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[dz]</span> or <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[zd]</span>. In Classical Latin poetry, the letter ⟨<span class="square-capitals" lang="la" style="font-family: Cardo, 'Everson Mono', 'GNU Unifont', Junicode; text-transform: uppercase; font-size: 73%;">z</span>⟩ between vowels always counts as two consonants for metrical purposes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Allen_Z_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-Allen_Z-52">[52]</a></sup> The consonant ⟨b⟩ usually sounds as [b]; however, when ⟨t⟩ or ⟨s⟩ follows ⟨b⟩ then it is pronounced as in [pt] or [ps]. Further, <a href="/wiki/Consonant" title="Consonant">consonants</a> do not blend together. So, ⟨ch⟩, ⟨ph⟩, and ⟨th⟩ are all sounds that would be pronounced as [kh], [ph], and [th]. In Latin, ⟨q⟩ is always followed by the <a href="/wiki/Vowel" title="Vowel">vowel</a> ⟨u⟩. Together they make a [kw] sound.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wheelock_7th_ed._53-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wheelock_7th_ed.-53">[53]</a></sup>
</p><p>In Old and Classical Latin, the Latin alphabet had no distinction between <a href="/wiki/Letter_case" title="Letter case">uppercase and lowercase</a>, and the letters ⟨J U W⟩ did not exist. In place of ⟨J U⟩, ⟨I V⟩ were used, respectively; ⟨I V⟩ represented both vowels and consonants. Most of the letterforms were similar to modern uppercase, as can be seen in the inscription from the Colosseum shown at the top of the article.
</p><p>The spelling systems used in Latin dictionaries and modern editions of Latin texts, however, normally use ⟨j u⟩ in place of Classical-era ⟨i v⟩. Some systems use ⟨j v⟩ for the consonant sounds <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/j w/</span> except in the combinations ⟨gu su qu⟩ for which ⟨v⟩ is never used.
</p><p>Some notes concerning the mapping of Latin phonemes to English graphemes are given below:
</p>
<table class="wikitable">
<caption>Notes

<th>Latin<br/>grapheme</th>

<tr>

</th>

</td></tr>

</td></tr>
<tr>
<th rowspan="2">⟨l⟩

</td></tr>
<tr>
