</th>
<td>In all other positions, as <a href="/wiki/Voiced_dental,_alveolar_and_postalveolar_lateral_approximants" title="Voiced dental, alveolar and postalveolar lateral approximants">"dark L"</a>, [ɫ] in <i>bowl</i> ([boʊɫ]) (<span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">l pinguis</i></span>)
</td></tr>
<tr>
<th>⟨qu⟩
</th>
<th><span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[kʷ]</span>
</th>
<td>Similar to <i>qu</i> in <i>squint</i> (/skwɪnt/)
</td></tr>
<tr>
<th>⟨u⟩
</th>
<th><span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[w]</span>
</th>
<td>Sometimes at the beginning of a syllable, or after ⟨g⟩ and ⟨s⟩, as /w/ in <i>wine</i> (/waɪn/)
</td></tr>
<tr>
<th rowspan="2">⟨i⟩
</th>
<th><span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[j]</span>
</th>
<td>Sometimes at the beginning of a syllable, as <i>y</i> (/j/) in <i>yard</i> (/jɑɹd/)
</td></tr>
<tr>
<th><span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ij]</span>
</th>
<td>"y" (/j/), in between vowels, becomes "i-y", being pronounced as parts of two separate syllables, as in capiō (/kapiˈjo:/)
</td></tr>
<tr>
<th>⟨x⟩
</th>
<th><span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ks]</span>
</th>
<td>A letter representing ⟨c⟩ + ⟨s⟩: as <i>x</i> in English <i>axe</i> (/æks/)
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>In Classical Latin, as in modern Italian, double consonant letters were pronounced as <a href="/wiki/Gemination" title="Gemination">long</a> consonant sounds distinct from short versions of the same consonants. Thus the <i>nn</i> in Classical Latin <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">annus</i></span> "year" (and in Italian <span title="Italian-language text"><i lang="it">anno</i></span>) is pronounced as a doubled <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/nn/</span> as in English <i>unnamed</i>. (In English, distinctive consonant length or doubling occurs only at the boundary between two words or <a href="/wiki/Morpheme" title="Morpheme">morphemes</a>, as in that example.)
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Vowels">Vowels</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Latin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Vowels">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Simple_vowels">Simple vowels</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Latin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Simple vowels">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<table class="wikitable" style="text-align: center;">
<tbody><tr>
<th></th>
<th>Front</th>
<th>Central</th>
<th>Back
</th></tr>
<tr>
<th>Close
</th>
<td><span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">iː ɪ</span></td>
<td></td>
<td><span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">ʊ uː</span>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<th>Mid
</th>
<td><span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">eː ɛ</span></td>
<td></td>
<td><span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">ɔ oː</span>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<th>Open
</th>
<td></td>
<td><span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">a aː</span></td>
<td>
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>In Classical Latin, ⟨U⟩ did not exist as a letter distinct from V; the written form ⟨V⟩ was used to represent both a vowel and a consonant. ⟨Y⟩ was adopted to represent <a href="/wiki/Upsilon" title="Upsilon">upsilon</a> in loanwords from <a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a>, but it was pronounced like ⟨u⟩ and ⟨i⟩ by some speakers. It was also used in native Latin words by confusion with Greek words of similar meaning, such as <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">sylva</i></span> and <span title="Ancient Greek (to 1453)-language text"><span lang="grc">ὕλη</span></span>.
</p><p>Classical Latin distinguished between <a href="/wiki/Vowel_length" title="Vowel length">long and short vowels</a>. Then, long vowels, except for ⟨I⟩, were frequently marked using the <a href="/wiki/Apex_(diacritic)" title="Apex (diacritic)">apex</a>, which was sometimes similar to an <a href="/wiki/Acute_accent" title="Acute accent">acute accent</a> ⟨Á É Ó V́ Ý⟩. Long <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/iː/</span> was written using a taller version of ⟨I⟩, called <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">i longa</i></span> "<a href="/wiki/Long_I" title="Long I">long I</a>": ⟨ꟾ⟩. In modern texts, long vowels are often indicated by a <a href="/wiki/Macron_(diacritic)" title="Macron (diacritic)">macron</a> ⟨ā ē ī ō ū⟩, and short vowels are usually unmarked except when it is necessary to distinguish between words, when they are marked with a <a href="/wiki/Breve" title="Breve">breve</a> ⟨ă ĕ ĭ ŏ ŭ⟩. However, they would also signify a long vowel by writing the vowel larger than other letters in a word or by repeating the vowel twice in a row.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wheelock_7th_ed._53-1"><a href="#cite_note-Wheelock_7th_ed.-53">[53]</a></sup> The acute accent, when it is used in modern Latin texts, indicates stress, as in Spanish, rather than length.
</p><p>Long vowels in Classical Latin are, technically, pronounced as entirely different from short vowels. The difference is described in the table below:
</p>
<table class="wikitable">
<caption>Pronunciation of Latin vowels
</caption>
<tbody><tr>
<th>Latin<br/>grapheme
</th>
<th>Latin<br/>phone
</th>
<th>modern examples
</th></tr>
<tr>
<th rowspan="2">⟨a⟩
</th>
<th><span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[a]</span>
</th>
<td>similar to the last <i>a</i> in <i>part</i> (/paɹt/)
</td></tr>
<tr>
<th><span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[aː]</span>
</th>
<td>similar to <i>a</i> in <i>father</i> (/fɑːðəɹ/)
</td></tr>
<tr>
<th rowspan="2">⟨e⟩
</th>
<th><span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ɛ]</span>

</td></tr>

</td></tr>
<tr>
<th rowspan="2">⟨i⟩
</th>

</th>
<td>as <i>i</i> in <i>pit</i> (/pɪt/)
</td></tr>
<tr>
<th><span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[iː]</span>
</th>
<td>similar to <i>i</i> in <i>machine</i> (/məʃiːn/)
</td></tr>
<tr>
<th rowspan="2">⟨o⟩
</th>
<th><span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ɔ]</span>
</th>
<td>as <i>o</i> in <i>port</i> (/pɔɹt/)
</td></tr>
<tr>
<th><span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[oː]</span>
</th>
<td>similar to <i>o</i> in <i>post</i> (/poʊst/)
</td></tr>
<tr>
<th rowspan="2">⟨u⟩
</th>
<th><span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ʊ]</span>
</th>
<td>as <i>u</i> in put (/pʊt/)
</td></tr>
<tr>
<th><span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[uː]</span>
</th>
<td>similar to <i>ue</i> in <i>true</i> (/tɹuː/)
</td></tr>
<tr>
<th rowspan="2">⟨y⟩
</th>
<th><span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ʏ]</span>
</th>
<td>does not exist in English; as <i>ü</i> in German <span title="German-language text"><i lang="de">Stück</i></span> (/ʃtʏk/)
</td></tr>
<tr>
<th><span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[yː]</span>
</th>
<td>does not exist in English; as <i>üh</i> in German <span title="German-language text"><i lang="de">früh</i></span> (/fʀyː/)
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>This difference in quality is posited by <a href="/wiki/W._Sidney_Allen" title="W. Sidney Allen">W. Sidney Allen</a> in his book <i>Vox Latina</i>. However, Andrea Calabrese has disputed that short vowels differed in quality from long vowels during the classical period, based in part upon the observation that in Sardinian and some Lucanian dialects, each long and short vowel pair was merged. This is distinguished from the typical Italo-Western romance vowel system in which short /i/ and /u/ merge with long /eː/ and /oː/. Thus, Latin 'siccus' becomes 'secco' in Italian and 'siccu' in Sardinian.
</p><p>A vowel letter followed by ⟨m⟩ at the end of a word, or a vowel letter followed by ⟨n⟩ before ⟨s⟩ or ⟨f⟩, represented a short <a href="/wiki/Nasal_vowel" title="Nasal vowel">nasal vowel</a>, as in <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">monstrum</i></span> <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[mõːstrũ]</span>.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Diphthongs">Diphthongs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Latin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Diphthongs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Classical Latin had several <a href="/wiki/Diphthong" title="Diphthong">diphthongs</a>. The two most common were ⟨ae au⟩. ⟨oe⟩ was fairly rare, and ⟨ui eu ei⟩ were very rare, at least in native Latin words.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-classical_diphthongs_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-classical_diphthongs-56">[56]</a></sup> There has also been debate over whether ⟨ui⟩ is truly a diphthong in Classical Latin, due to its rarity, absence in works of Roman grammarians, and the roots of Classical Latin words (i.e. <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">hui ce</i></span> to <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">huic</i></span>, <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">quoi</i></span> to <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">cui</i></span>, etc.) not matching or being similar to the pronunciation of classical words if ⟨ui⟩ were to be considered a diphthong.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup>
</p><p>The sequences sometimes did not represent diphthongs. ⟨ae⟩ and ⟨oe⟩ also represented a sequence of two vowels in different syllables in <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">aēnus</i></span> <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[aˈeː.nʊs]</span> "of bronze" and <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">coēpit</i></span> <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[kɔˈeː.pɪt]</span> "began", and ⟨au ui eu ei ou⟩ represented sequences of two vowels or of a vowel and one of the semivowels <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/j w/</span>, in <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">cavē</i></span> <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ˈka.weː]</span> "beware!", <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">cuius</i></span> <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ˈkʊj.jʊs]</span> "whose", <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">monuī</i></span> <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ˈmɔn.ʊ.iː]</span> "I warned", <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">solvī</i></span> <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ˈsɔɫ.wiː]</span> "I released", <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">dēlēvī</i></span> <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[deːˈleː.wiː]</span> "I destroyed", <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">eius</i></span> <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ˈɛj.jʊs]</span> "his", and <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">novus</i></span> <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ˈnɔ.wʊs]</span> "new".
</p><p>Old Latin had more diphthongs, but most of them changed into long vowels in Classical Latin. The Old Latin diphthong ⟨ai⟩ and the sequence ⟨āī⟩ became Classical ⟨ae⟩. Old Latin ⟨oi⟩ and ⟨ou⟩ changed to Classical ⟨ū⟩, except in a few words whose ⟨oi⟩ became Classical ⟨oe⟩. These two developments sometimes occurred in different words from the same root: for instance, Classical <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">poena</i></span> "punishment" and <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">pūnīre</i></span> "to punish".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-classical_diphthongs_56-1"><a href="#cite_note-classical_diphthongs-56">[56]</a></sup> Early Old Latin ⟨ei⟩ usually changed to Classical ⟨ī⟩.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup>
</p><p>In Vulgar Latin and the Romance languages, ⟨ae oe⟩ merged with ⟨e ē⟩. During the Classical Latin period this form of speaking was deliberately avoided by well-educated speakers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-classical_diphthongs_56-2"><a href="#cite_note-classical_diphthongs-56">[56]</a></sup>
</p>
<table class="wikitable" style="text-align: center;">
<caption>Diphthongs classified by beginning sound
</caption>
<tbody><tr>
<th></th>
<th>Front</th>
<th>Back
</th></tr>
<tr>

</th>
<td></td>
<td>ui <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ui̯/</span>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<th>Mid
</th>
<td>ei <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ei̯/</span><br/>eu <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/eu̯/</span></td>
<td>oe <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/oe̯/</span><br/>ou <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ou̯/</span>
</td></tr>
<tr>
<th>Open
</th>
<td colspan="2">ae <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ae̯/</span><br/>au <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/au̯/</span>
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Syllables">Syllables</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Latin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Syllables">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Syllable" title="Syllable">Syllables</a> in Latin are signified by the presence of diphthongs and <a href="/wiki/Vowel" title="Vowel">vowels</a>. The number of syllables is the same as the number of vowel sounds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wheelock_7th_ed._53-2"><a href="#cite_note-Wheelock_7th_ed.-53">[53]</a></sup>
</p><p>Further, if a consonant separates two vowels, it will go into the syllable of the second vowel. When there are two consonants between vowels, the last consonant will go with the second vowel. An exception occurs when a <a href="/wiki/Phonetic_transcription" title="Phonetic transcription">phonetic</a> stop and liquid come together. In this situation, they are thought to be a single consonant, and as such, they will go into the syllable of the second vowel.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wheelock_7th_ed._53-3"><a href="#cite_note-Wheelock_7th_ed.-53">[53]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Length">Length</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Latin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Length">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Syllables in Latin are considered either <a href="/wiki/Syllable_weight#Basic_definitions" title="Syllable weight">long or short</a>. Within a word, a syllable may either be long by nature or long by position.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wheelock_7th_ed._53-4"><a href="#cite_note-Wheelock_7th_ed.-53">[53]</a></sup> A syllable is long by nature if it has a diphthong or a long vowel. On the other hand, a syllable is long by position if the vowel is followed by more than one consonant.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wheelock_7th_ed._53-5"><a href="#cite_note-Wheelock_7th_ed.-53">[53]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Stress">Stress</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Latin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Stress">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>There are two rules that define which syllable is <a href="/wiki/Stress_(linguistics)" title="Stress (linguistics)">stressed</a> in the Latin language.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wheelock_7th_ed._53-6"><a href="#cite_note-Wheelock_7th_ed.-53">[53]</a></sup>
</p>
<ol><li>In a word with only two syllables, the emphasis will be on the first syllable.</li>
<li>In a word with more than two syllables, there are two cases.
<ul><li>If the second-to-last syllable is long, that syllable will have stress.</li>
<li>If the second-to-last syllable is not long, the syllable before that one will be stressed instead.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wheelock_7th_ed._53-7"><a href="#cite_note-Wheelock_7th_ed.-53">[53]</a></sup></li></ul></li></ol>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Orthography">Orthography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Latin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Orthography">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Latin_alphabet" title="Latin alphabet">Latin alphabet</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Duenos_inscription.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="464" data-file-width="448" decoding="async" height="228" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/Duenos_inscription.jpg/220px-Duenos_inscription.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/Duenos_inscription.jpg/330px-Duenos_inscription.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/33/Duenos_inscription.jpg/440px-Duenos_inscription.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Duenos_inscription.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Duenos_Inscription" title="Duenos Inscription">Duenos Inscription</a>, from the 6th century BC, is one of the earliest known <a href="/wiki/Old_Latin" title="Old Latin">Old Latin</a> texts. It was found on the <a href="/wiki/Quirinal_Hill" title="Quirinal Hill">Quirinal Hill</a> in Rome.</div></div></div>
<p>Latin was written in the Latin alphabet, derived from the <a href="/wiki/Etruscan_alphabet" title="Etruscan alphabet">Etruscan alphabet</a>, which was in turn drawn from the <a href="/wiki/Greek_alphabet" title="Greek alphabet">Greek alphabet</a> and ultimately the <a href="/wiki/Phoenician_alphabet" title="Phoenician alphabet">Phoenician alphabet</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> This alphabet has continued to be used over the centuries as the script for the Romance, Celtic, Germanic, Baltic, Finnic and many Slavic languages (<a href="/wiki/Polish_language" title="Polish language">Polish</a>, <a href="/wiki/Slovak_language" title="Slovak language">Slovak</a>, <a href="/wiki/Slovene_language" title="Slovene language">Slovene</a>, <a href="/wiki/Croatian_language" title="Croatian language">Croatian</a>, <a href="/wiki/Bosnian_language" title="Bosnian language">Bosnian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Czech_language" title="Czech language">Czech</a>); and it has been adopted by many languages around the world, including <a href="/wiki/Vietnamese_language" title="Vietnamese language">Vietnamese</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Austronesian_languages" title="Austronesian languages">Austronesian languages</a>, many <a href="/wiki/Turkic_languages" title="Turkic languages">Turkic languages</a>, and most languages in <a href="/wiki/Sub-Saharan_Africa" title="Sub-Saharan Africa">sub-Saharan Africa</a>, the Americas and Oceania, making it by far the world's single most widely used writing system.
</p><p>The number of letters in the Latin alphabet has varied. When it was first derived from the Etruscan alphabet, it contained only 21 letters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup> Later, <i>G</i> was added to represent <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/ɡ/</span>, which had previously been spelled <i>C</i>, and <i>Z</i> ceased to be included in the alphabet, as the language then had no <a href="/wiki/Voiced_alveolar_fricative" title="Voiced alveolar fricative">voiced alveolar fricative</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-D538_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-D538-61">[61]</a></sup> The letters <i>Y</i> and <i>Z</i> were later added to represent Greek letters, <a href="/wiki/Upsilon" title="Upsilon">upsilon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Zeta" title="Zeta">zeta</a> respectively, in Greek loanwords.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-D538_61-1"><a href="#cite_note-D538-61">[61]</a></sup>
</p><p><i>W</i> was created in the 11th century from <i>VV</i>. It represented <span class="IPA" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/w/</span> in Germanic languages, not Latin, which still uses <i>V</i> for the purpose. <i>J</i> was distinguished from the original <i>I</i> only during the late Middle Ages, as was the letter <i>U</i> from <i>V</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-D538_61-2"><a href="#cite_note-D538-61">[61]</a></sup> Although some Latin dictionaries use <i>J</i>, it is rarely used for Latin text, as it was not used in classical times, but many other languages use it.
</p><p>Classical Latin did not contain sentence <a href="/wiki/Punctuation" title="Punctuation">punctuation</a>, letter case,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Interword_spacing" title="Interword spacing">interword spacing</a>, but <a href="/wiki/Apex_(diacritic)" title="Apex (diacritic)">apices</a> were sometimes used to distinguish length in vowels and the <a href="/wiki/Interpunct" title="Interpunct">interpunct</a> was used at times to separate words. The first line of Catullus 3, originally written as
</p>
<dl><dd><style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r920966791">.mw-parser-output span.smallcaps{font-variant:small-caps}.mw-parser-output span.smallcaps-smaller{font-size:85%}</style><span class="smallcaps">lv́géteóveneréscupidinésqve</span> ("Mourn, O <a href="/wiki/Venus_(mythology)" title="Venus (mythology)">Venuses</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cupid" title="Cupid">Cupids</a>")</dd></dl>
<p>or with <a href="/wiki/Long_I" title="Long I">long I</a> as
</p><p><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r920966791" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="smallcaps">lv́géteóveneréscupꟾdinésqve</span>
</p><p>or with interpunct as
</p>
<dl><dd><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r920966791" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><span class="smallcaps">lv́géte·ó·venerés·cupidinésqve</span></dd></dl>
<p>would be rendered in a modern edition as
</p>
<dl><dd>Lugete, o Veneres Cupidinesque</dd></dl>
<p>or with macrons
</p>
<dl><dd>Lūgēte, ō Venerēs Cupīdinēsque</dd></dl>
<p>or with apices
</p>
<dl><dd>Lúgéte, ó Venerés Cupídinésque.</dd></dl>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:292px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Hocgracili.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="182" data-file-width="691" decoding="async" height="76" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Hocgracili.jpg/290px-Hocgracili.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Hocgracili.jpg/435px-Hocgracili.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7d/Hocgracili.jpg/580px-Hocgracili.jpg 2x" width="290"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Hocgracili.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A modern Latin text written in the Old Roman Cursive inspired by the <a href="/wiki/Vindolanda_tablets" title="Vindolanda tablets">Vindolanda tablets</a>, the oldest surviving handwritten documents in Britain. The word <i>Romani</i> ('Romans') is at bottom left.</div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Roman_cursive" title="Roman cursive">Roman cursive</a> script is commonly found on the many <a href="/wiki/Wax_tablet" title="Wax tablet">wax tablets</a> excavated at sites such as forts, an especially extensive set having been discovered at Vindolanda on <a href="/wiki/Hadrian%27s_Wall" title="Hadrian's Wall">Hadrian's Wall</a> in <a href="/wiki/Great_Britain" title="Great Britain">Britain</a>. Most notable is the fact that while most of the <a href="/wiki/Vindolanda_tablets" title="Vindolanda tablets">Vindolanda tablets</a> show spaces between words, spaces were avoided in monumental inscriptions from that era.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Alternative_scripts">Alternative scripts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Latin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Alternative scripts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Occasionally, Latin has been written in other scripts:
</p>
<ul><li>The <a href="/wiki/Praeneste_fibula" title="Praeneste fibula">Praeneste fibula</a> is a 7th-century BC pin with an Old Latin inscription written using the Etruscan script.</li>
<li>The rear panel of the early 8th-century <a href="/wiki/Franks_Casket" title="Franks Casket">Franks Casket</a> has an inscription that switches from <a href="/wiki/Old_English" title="Old English">Old English</a> in <a href="/wiki/Anglo-Saxon_runes" title="Anglo-Saxon runes">Anglo-Saxon runes</a> to Latin in Latin script and to Latin in runes.</li></ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Grammar">Grammar</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Latin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Grammar">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Latin_grammar" title="Latin grammar">Latin grammar</a> and <a href="/wiki/Latin_syntax" title="Latin syntax">Latin syntax</a></div>
<p>Latin is a <a href="/wiki/Synthetic_language" title="Synthetic language">synthetic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fusional_language" title="Fusional language">fusional language</a> in the terminology of linguistic typology. In more traditional terminology, it is an inflected language, but typologists are apt to say "inflecting". Words include an objective semantic element and markers specifying the grammatical use of the word. The fusion of root meaning and markers produces very compact sentence elements: <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">amō</i></span>, "I love," is produced from a semantic element, <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">ama-</i></span>, "love," to which <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">-ō</i></span>, a first person singular marker, is suffixed.
</p><p>The grammatical function can be changed by changing the markers: the word is "inflected" to express different grammatical functions, but the semantic element usually does not change. (Inflection uses affixing and infixing. Affixing is prefixing and suffixing. Latin inflections are never prefixed.)
</p><p>For example, <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">amābit</i></span>, "he (or she or it) will love", is formed from the same stem, <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">amā-</i></span>, to which a future tense marker, <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">-bi-</i></span>, is suffixed, and a third person singular marker, <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">-t</i></span>, is suffixed. There is an inherent ambiguity: <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">-t</i></span> may denote more than one grammatical category: masculine, feminine, or neuter gender. A major task in understanding Latin phrases and clauses is to clarify such ambiguities by an analysis of context. All natural languages contain ambiguities of one sort or another.
</p><p>The inflections express <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_gender" title="Grammatical gender">gender</a>, <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_number" title="Grammatical number">number</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_case" title="Grammatical case">case</a> in <a href="/wiki/Adjective" title="Adjective">adjectives</a>, <a href="/wiki/Noun" title="Noun">nouns</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Pronoun" title="Pronoun">pronouns</a>, a process called <i><a href="/wiki/Declension" title="Declension">declension</a></i>. Markers are also attached to fixed stems of verbs, to denote <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_person" title="Grammatical person">person</a>, <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_number" title="Grammatical number">number</a>, <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_tense" title="Grammatical tense">tense</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Grammatical_voice" title="Grammatical voice">voice</a>, <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_mood" title="Grammatical mood">mood</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_aspect" title="Grammatical aspect">aspect</a>, a process called <i><a href="/wiki/Grammatical_conjugation" title="Grammatical conjugation">conjugation</a></i>. Some words are uninflected and undergo neither process, such as adverbs, prepositions, and interjections.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Nouns">Nouns</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Latin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Nouns">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Latin_declension" title="Latin declension">Latin declension</a></div>
<p>A regular Latin noun belongs to one of five main declensions, a group of nouns with similar inflected forms. The declensions are identified by the genitive singular form of the noun.
</p>
<ul><li>The first declension, with a predominant ending letter of <i>a</i>, is signified by the genitive singular ending of <i>-ae</i>.</li>
<li>The second declension, with a predominant ending letter of <i>us</i>, is signified by the genitive singular ending of <i>-i</i>.</li>
<li>The third declension, with a predominant ending letter of <i>i</i>, is signified by the genitive singular ending of <i>-is</i>.</li>
<li>The fourth declension, with a predominant ending letter of <i>u</i>, is signified by the genitive singular ending of <i>-ūs</i>.</li>
<li>The fifth declension, with a predominant ending letter of <i>e</i>, is signified by the genitive singular ending of <i>-ei</i>.</li></ul>
<p>There are seven Latin noun cases, which also apply to adjectives and pronouns and mark a noun's syntactic role in the sentence by means of inflections. Thus, <a href="/wiki/Word_order" title="Word order">word order</a> is not as important in Latin as it is in English, which is less inflected. The general structure and word order of a Latin sentence can therefore vary. The cases are as follows:
</p>
<ol><li><a href="/wiki/Nominative_case" title="Nominative case">Nominative</a> – used when the noun is the <a href="/wiki/Subject_(grammar)" title="Subject (grammar)">subject</a> or a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Predicate_nominative" title="Predicate nominative">predicate nominative</a>. The thing or person acting: the girl ran: <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">puella cucurrit,</i></span> or <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">cucurrit puella</i></span></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Genitive_case" title="Genitive case">Genitive</a> – used when the noun is the possessor of or connected with an object: "the horse of the man", or "the man's horse"; in both instances, the word <i>man</i> would be in the <a href="/wiki/Genitive_case" title="Genitive case">genitive case</a> when it is translated into Latin. It also indicates the <a href="/wiki/Partitive" title="Partitive">partitive</a>, in which the material is quantified: "a group of people"; "a number of gifts": <i>people</i> and <i>gifts</i> would be in the genitive case. Some nouns are genitive with special verbs and adjectives: The cup is full of wine. (<span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">Poculum plēnum vīnī est.</i></span>) The master of the slave had beaten him. (<span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">Dominus servī eum verberāverat.</i></span>)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Dative_case" title="Dative case">Dative</a> – used when the noun is the indirect object of the sentence, with special verbs, with certain prepositions, and if it is used as agent, reference, or even possessor: The merchant hands the <a href="/wiki/Stola" title="Stola">stola</a> to the woman. (<span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">Mercātor fēminae stolam trādit.</i></span>)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Accusative_case" title="Accusative case">Accusative</a> – used when the noun is the direct object of the subject and as the object of a preposition demonstrating place to which.: The man killed the boy. (<span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">Vir puerum necāvit.</i></span>)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Ablative_case" title="Ablative case">Ablative</a> – used when the noun demonstrates separation or movement from a source, cause, <a href="/wiki/Agent_(grammar)" title="Agent (grammar)">agent</a> or <a href="/wiki/Instrumental_case" title="Instrumental case">instrument</a> or when the noun is used as the object of certain prepositions; adverbial: You walked with the boy. (<span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">Cum puerō ambulāvistī.</i></span>)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Vocative_case" title="Vocative case">Vocative</a> – used when the noun is used in a direct address. The vocative form of a noun is often the same as the nominative, with the exception of second-declension nouns ending in <i>-us</i>. The <i>-us</i> becomes an <i>-e</i> in the vocative singular. If it ends in <i>-ius</i> (such as <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">fīlius</i></span>), the ending is just <i>-ī</i> (<span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">filī</i></span>), as distinct from the nominative plural (<span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">filiī</i></span>) in the vocative singular: "Master!" shouted the slave. (<span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">"Domine!" clāmāvit servus.</i></span>)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Locative_case" title="Locative case">Locative</a> – used to indicate a location (corresponding to the English "in" or "at"). It is far less common than the other six cases of Latin nouns and usually applies to cities and small towns and islands along with a few common nouns, such as the words <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">domus</i></span> (house), <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">humus</i></span> (ground), and <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">rus</i></span> (country). In the singular of the first and second declensions, its form coincides with the genitive (<span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">Roma</i></span> becomes <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">Romae</i></span>, "in Rome"). In the plural of all declensions and the singular of the other declensions, it coincides with the ablative (<span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">Athēnae</i></span> becomes <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">Athēnīs</i></span>, "at Athens"). In the fourth-declension word <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">domus</i></span>, the locative form, <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">domī</i></span> ("at home") differs from the standard form of all other cases.</li></ol>
<p>Latin lacks both definite and indefinite <a href="/wiki/Article_(grammar)" title="Article (grammar)">articles</a> so <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">puer currit</i></span> can mean either "the boy is running" or "a boy is running".
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Adjectives">Adjectives</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Latin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: Adjectives">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Latin_declension#Adjectives" title="Latin declension">Latin declension § Adjectives</a></div>
<p>There are two types of regular Latin adjectives: first- and second-declension and third-declension. They are so-called because their forms are similar or identical to first- and second-declension and third-declension nouns, respectively. Latin adjectives also have <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Comparative_and_superlative" title="Comparative and superlative">comparative and superlative</a> forms. There are also a number of Latin <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Participles" title="Participles">participles</a>.
</p><p>Latin numbers are sometimes declined as adjectives. See <i><a href="/wiki/Latin#Numbers" title="Latin">Numbers</a></i> below.
</p><p><em>First- and second-declension adjectives</em> are declined like first-declension nouns for the feminine forms and like second-declension nouns for the masculine and neuter forms. For example, for <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">mortuus, mortua, mortuum</i></span> (dead), <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">mortua</i></span> is declined like a regular first-declension noun (such as <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">puella</i></span> (girl)), <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">mortuus</i></span> is declined like a regular second-declension masculine noun (such as <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">dominus</i></span> (lord, master)), and <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">mortuum</i></span> is declined like a regular second-declension neuter noun (such as <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">auxilium</i></span> (help)).
</p><p><em>Third-declension adjectives</em> are mostly declined like normal third-declension nouns, with a few exceptions. In the plural nominative neuter, for example, the ending is <i>-ia</i> (<span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">omnia</i></span> (all, everything)), and for third-declension nouns, the plural nominative neuter ending is <i>-a</i> or <i>-ia</i> (<span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">capita</i></span> (heads), <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">animalia</i></span> (animals)) They can have one, two or three forms for the masculine, feminine, and neuter nominative singular.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Participles">Participles</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Latin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Participles">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Latin participles, like English participles, are formed from a verb. There are a few main types of participles: Present Active Participles, Perfect Passive Participles, Future Active Participles, and Future Passive Participles.
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Prepositions">Prepositions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Latin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Prepositions">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Latin sometimes uses prepositions, depending on the type of prepositional phrase being used. Most prepositions are followed by a noun in either the accusative or ablative case: "apud puerum" (with the boy), with "puerum" being the accusative form of "puer", boy, and "sine puero" (without the boy), "puero" being the ablative form of "puer". A few <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Adposition" title="Adposition">adpositions</a>, however, govern a noun in the genitive (such as "gratia" and "tenus").
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Verbs">Verbs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Latin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Verbs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Latin_grammar" title="Latin grammar">Latin grammar</a> and <a href="/wiki/Latin_conjugation" title="Latin conjugation">Latin conjugation</a></div>
<p>A regular verb in Latin belongs to one of four main <a href="/wiki/Latin_conjugation" title="Latin conjugation">conjugations</a>. A conjugation is "a class of verbs with similar inflected forms."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup> The conjugations are identified by the last letter of the verb's present stem. The present stem can be found by omitting the -<i>re</i> (-<i>rī</i> in deponent verbs) ending from the present infinitive form. The infinitive of the first conjugation ends in <i>-ā-re</i> or <i>-ā-ri</i> (active and passive respectively): <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">amāre</i></span>, "to love," <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">hortārī</i></span>, "to exhort"; of the second conjugation by <i>-ē-re</i> or <i>-ē-rī</i>: <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">monēre</i></span>, "to warn", <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">verērī</i></span>, "to fear;" of the third conjugation by <i>-ere</i>, <i>-ī</i>: <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">dūcere</i></span>, "to lead," <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">ūtī</i></span>, "to use"; of the fourth by <i>-ī-re</i>, <i>-ī-rī</i>: <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">audīre</i></span>, "to hear," <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">experīrī</i></span>, "to attempt".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wheelock_2011_64-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wheelock_2011-64">[64]</a></sup> The stem categories descend from <a href="/wiki/Proto-Indo-European_language" title="Proto-Indo-European language">Indo-European</a> and can therefore be compared to similar conjugations in other Indo-European languages.
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Regular_and_irregular_verbs" title="Regular and irregular verbs">Irregular verbs</a> are verbs that do not follow the regular conjugations in the formation of the inflected form. Irregular verbs in Latin are <i>esse</i>, "to be"; <i>velle</i>, "to want"; <i>ferre</i>, "to carry"; <i>edere</i>, "to eat"; <i>dare</i>, "to give"; <i>ire</i>, "to go"; <i>posse</i>, "to be able"; <i>fieri</i>, "to happen"; and their compounds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wheelock_2011_64-1"><a href="#cite_note-Wheelock_2011-64">[64]</a></sup>
</p><p>There are six general <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_tense" title="Grammatical tense">tenses</a> in Latin (present, imperfect, future, perfect, pluperfect and future perfect), three <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_mood" title="Grammatical mood">moods</a> (indicative, imperative and subjunctive, in addition to the <a href="/wiki/Infinitive" title="Infinitive">infinitive</a>, <a href="/wiki/Participle" title="Participle">participle</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gerund" title="Gerund">gerund</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gerundive" title="Gerundive">gerundive</a> and <a href="/wiki/Supine" title="Supine">supine</a>), three <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_person" title="Grammatical person">persons</a> (first, second and third), two numbers (singular and plural), two <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Grammatical_voice" title="Grammatical voice">voices</a> (active and passive) and two <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_aspect" title="Grammatical aspect">aspects</a> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Perfective_and_imperfective" title="Perfective and imperfective">perfective and imperfective</a>). Verbs are described by four principal parts:
</p>
<ol><li>The first principal part is the first-person singular, present tense, active voice, indicative mood form of the verb. If the verb is impersonal, the first principal part will be in the third-person singular.</li>
<li>The second principal part is the present active infinitive.</li>
<li>The third principal part is the first-person singular, perfect active indicative form. Like the first principal part, if the verb is impersonal, the third principal part will be in the third-person singular.</li>
<li>The fourth principal part is the supine form, or alternatively, the nominative singular of the perfect passive participle form of the verb. The fourth principal part can show one gender of the participle or all three genders (-<i>us </i>for masculine, -<i>a</i> for feminine and -<i>um</i> for neuter) in the nominative singular. The fourth principal part will be the future participle if the verb cannot be made passive. Most modern Latin dictionaries, if they show only one gender, tend to show the masculine; but many older dictionaries instead show the neuter, as it coincides with the supine. The fourth principal part is sometimes omitted for intransitive verbs, but strictly in Latin, they can be made passive if they are used impersonally, and the supine exists for such verbs.</li></ol>
<p>The six tenses of Latin are divided into two tense systems: the present system, which is made up of the present, imperfect and future tenses, and the perfect system, which is made up of the perfect, pluperfect and future perfect tenses. Each tense has a set of endings corresponding to the person, number, and voice of the subject. Subject (nominative) pronouns are generally omitted for the first (<i>I, we</i>) and second (<i>you</i>) persons except for emphasis.
</p><p>The table below displays the common inflected endings for the indicative mood in the active voice in all six tenses. For the future tense, the first listed endings are for the first and second conjugations, and the second listed endings are for the third and fourth conjugations:
</p>
<table class="wikitable">

<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Deponent_verbs">Deponent verbs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Latin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: Deponent verbs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Some Latin verbs are <a href="/wiki/Deponent_verb" title="Deponent verb">deponent</a>, causing their forms to be in the passive voice but retain an active meaning: <i>hortor, hortārī, hortātus sum</i> (to urge).
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Vocabulary">Vocabulary</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Latin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Vocabulary">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>As Latin is an Italic language, most of its vocabulary is likewise Italic, ultimately from the ancestral <a href="/wiki/Proto-Indo-European_language" title="Proto-Indo-European language">Proto-Indo-European language</a>. However, because of close cultural interaction, the Romans not only adapted the Etruscan alphabet to form the Latin alphabet but also borrowed some <a href="/wiki/Etruscan_language" title="Etruscan language">Etruscan</a> words into their language, including <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">persona</i></span> "mask" and <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">histrio</i></span> "actor".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-H&amp;S13_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-H&amp;S13-65">[65]</a></sup> Latin also included vocabulary borrowed from <a href="/wiki/Oscan_language" title="Oscan language">Oscan</a>, another Italic language.
</p><p>After the <a href="/wiki/History_of_Taranto" title="History of Taranto">Fall of Tarentum</a> (272 BC), the Romans began Hellenising, or adopting features of Greek culture, including the borrowing of Greek words, such as <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">camera</i></span> (vaulted roof), <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">sumbolum</i></span> (symbol), and <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">balineum</i></span> (bath).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-H&amp;S13_65-1"><a href="#cite_note-H&amp;S13-65">[65]</a></sup> This Hellenisation led to the addition of "Y" and "Z" to the alphabet to represent Greek sounds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup> Subsequently, the Romans transplanted <a href="/wiki/Greek_art" title="Greek art">Greek art</a>, medicine, science and philosophy to Italy, paying almost any price to entice Greek skilled and educated persons to Rome and sending their youth to be educated in Greece. Thus, many Latin scientific and philosophical words were Greek loanwords or had their meanings expanded by association with Greek words, as <span title="Greek-language text"><i lang="el">ars</i></span> (craft) and τέχνη (art).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-H&amp;S14_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-H&amp;S14-67">[67]</a></sup>
</p><p>Because of the Roman Empire's expansion and subsequent trade with outlying European tribes, the Romans borrowed some northern and central European words, such as <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">beber</i></span> (beaver), of Germanic origin, and <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">bracae</i></span> (breeches), of Celtic origin.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-H&amp;S14_67-1"><a href="#cite_note-H&amp;S14-67">[67]</a></sup> The specific dialects of Latin across Latin-speaking regions of the former Roman Empire after its fall were influenced by languages specific to the regions. The dialects of Latin evolved into different Romance languages.
</p><p>During and after the adoption of Christianity into Roman society, Christian vocabulary became a part of the language, either from Greek or Hebrew borrowings or as Latin neologisms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> Continuing into the Middle Ages, Latin incorporated many more words from surrounding languages, including <a href="/wiki/Old_English" title="Old English">Old English</a> and other <a href="/wiki/Germanic_languages" title="Germanic languages">Germanic languages</a>.
</p><p>Over the ages, Latin-speaking populations produced new adjectives, nouns, and verbs by <a href="/wiki/Affix" title="Affix">affixing</a> or <a href="/wiki/Compound_(linguistics)" title="Compound (linguistics)">compounding</a> meaningful <a href="/wiki/Segment_(linguistics)" title="Segment (linguistics)">segments</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> For example, the compound adjective, <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">omnipotens</i></span>, "all-powerful," was produced from the adjectives <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">omnis</i></span>, "all", and <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">potens</i></span>, "powerful", by dropping the final <i>s</i> of <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">omnis</i></span> and concatenating. Often, the concatenation changed the part of speech, and nouns were produced from verb segments or verbs from nouns and adjectives.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span id="Phrases_.28Neo-Latin.29"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Phrases_(Neo-Latin)">Phrases (Neo-Latin)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Latin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Phrases (Neo-Latin)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1097763485">.mw-parser-output .ambox{border:1px solid #a2a9b1;border-left:10px solid #36c;background-color:#fbfbfb;box-sizing:border-box}.mw-parser-output .mbox-text-span{margin-left:23px!important}@media(min-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .ambox{margin:0 10%}}</style><table class="box-MOS plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-style ambox-mos" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div class="mbox-image-div"><img alt="" data-file-height="48" data-file-width="48" decoding="async" height="40" compliance with Wikipedia's <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style">Manual of Style</a>.</b> In particular, it has problems with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/MOS:WORDSASWORDS" title="MOS:WORDSASWORDS">MOS:WORDSASWORDS</a>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Latin&amp;action=edit">help improve it</a> if you can.</span> <span class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">August 2022</span>)</i></span><span class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<small><a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a></small>)</i></span></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_Latin_phrases" title="List of Latin phrases">List of Latin phrases</a></div>
<p>The phrases are mentioned with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pitch_accent" title="Pitch accent">accents</a> to show where stress is placed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup> In Latin, words are normally stressed either on the second-to-last (penultimate) <a href="/wiki/Syllable" title="Syllable">syllable</a>, called in Latin <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">paenultima</i></span> or <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">syllaba paenultima</i></span>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LKHS_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-LKHS-72">[72]</a></sup> or on the third-to-last syllable, called in Latin <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">antepaenultima</i></span> or <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">syllaba antepaenultima</i></span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LKHS_72-1"><a href="#cite_note-LKHS-72">[72]</a></sup> In the following notation, accented short vowels have an acute diacritic, accented long vowels have a <a href="/wiki/Circumflex" title="Circumflex">circumflex</a> diacritic (representing long falling pitch), and unaccented long vowels are marked simply with a macron. This reflects the tone of the voice with which, ideally, the stress is phonetically realized; but this may not always be clearly articulated on every word in a sentence.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> Regardless of length, a vowel at the end of a word may be significantly shortened or even altogether deleted if the next word begins with a vowel also (a process called elision), unless a very short pause is inserted. As an exception, the following words: <i>est</i> (English "is"), <i>es</i> ("[you (sg.)] are") lose their own vowel <i>e</i> instead.
</p><p><span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">salvē</i></span> <small>to one person</small> / <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">salvēte</i></span> <small>to more than one person</small> – hello
</p><p><span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">havē</i></span> <small>to one person</small> / <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">havēte</i></span> <small>to more than one person</small> – greetings
</p><p><span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">valē</i></span> <small>to one person</small> / <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">valēte</i></span> <small>to more than one person</small> – goodbye
</p><p><span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">cūrā ut valeās</i></span> – take care
</p><p><span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">exoptātus</i></span> <small>to male</small> / <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">exoptāta</i></span> <small>to female</small>, <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">optātus</i></span> <small>to male</small> / <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">optāta</i></span> <small>to female</small>, <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">grātus</i></span> <small>to male</small> / <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">grāta</i></span> <small>to female</small>, <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">acceptus</i></span> <small>to male</small> / <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">accepta</i></span> <small>to female</small> – welcome
</p><p><span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">quōmodo valēs?</i></span>, <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">ut válēs?</i></span> – how are you?
</p><p><span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">bene</i></span> – good
</p><p><span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">bene valeō</i></span> – I'm fine
</p><p><span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">male</i></span> – bad
</p><p><span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">male valeō</i></span> – I'm not good
</p><p><span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">quaesō</i></span> (roughly: ['kwaeso:]/['kwe:so:]) – please
</p><p><span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">amābō tē</i></span> – please
</p><p><span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">ita</i></span>, <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">ita est</i></span>, <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">ita vērō</i></span>, <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">sī</i></span>, <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">sīc est</i></span>, <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">etiam</i></span> – yes
</p><p><span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">nōn</i></span>, <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">minimē</i></span> – no
</p><p><span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">grātiās tibi</i></span>, <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">grātiās tibi agō</i></span> – thank you, I give thanks to you
</p><p><span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">magnās grātiās</i></span>, <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">magnās grātiās agō</i></span> – many thanks
</p><p><span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">maximās grātiās</i></span>, <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">maximās grātiās agō</i></span>, <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">ingentēs grātiās agō</i></span> – thank you very much
</p><p><span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">accipe sīs</i></span> <small>to one person</small> / <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">accipite sītis</i></span> <small>to more than one person</small>, <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">libenter</i></span> – you're welcome
</p><p><span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">quā aetāte es?</i></span> – how old are you?
</p><p>25 (vīgintī quīnque) annōs nātus sum <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la"><small>by male</small> /</i></span>25 annōs nāta sum <small>by female</small> – I am 25 years old
</p><p><span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">ubi lātrīna est?</i></span> – where is the toilet?
</p><p><span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">scīs (tū) ...</i></span> – do you speak (literally: "do you know") ... 
</p>
<ul><li><span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">Latīnē?</i></span> – Latin?</li>
<li><span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">Graecē?</i></span> – Greek?</li>
<li><span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">Anglicē?</i></span> – English?</li>
<li><span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">Theodiscē?</i></span>/<span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">Germānicē?</i></span> – German? (sometimes also: <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">Teutonicē</i></span>)</li>
<li><span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">Gallo-romanicē?</i></span> – French?</li>
<li><span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">Russicē?</i></span>/<span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">Ruthēnicē</i></span> – Russian?</li>
<li><span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">Italiānē?</i></span> – Italian?</li>
<li><span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">Hispānicē?</i></span>/<span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">Castellanicē?</i></span> – Spanish?</li>
<li><span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">Polonicē?</i></span> – Polish?</li>
<li><span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">Lūsītānē?</i></span> – Portuguese?</li>
<li><span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">Dāco-rōmānice?</i></span> – Romanian?</li>
<li><span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">Suēcicē?</i></span> – Swedish?</li>
<li><span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">Cambricē?</i></span> – Welsh?</li>
<li><span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">Sīnicē?</i></span> – Chinese?</li>
<li><span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">Iapōnicē?</i></span> – Japanese?</li>
<li><span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">Corēānē?</i></span> – Korean?</li>
<li><span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">Hebraicē?</i></span> – Hebrew?</li>
<li><span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">Arabicē?</i></span> – Arabic?</li>
<li><span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">Persicē?</i></span> – Persian?</li>
<li><span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">Hindicē?</i></span> – Hindi?</li>
<li><i>Bengalicē?</i> – Bengali?</li></ul>
<p><span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">amō tē</i></span> / <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">tē amō</i></span> – I love you
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Numbers">Numbers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Latin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: Numbers">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1097763485" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><table class="box-Unreferenced_section plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Unreferenced" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div class="mbox-image-div"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="399" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="39" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" width="50"/></a></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>does not <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources">cite</a> any <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">sources</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help <a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Latin&amp;action=edit">improve this section</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Referencing_for_beginners" title="Help:Referencing for beginners">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability#Burden_of_evidence" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">removed</a>.</span> <span class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">May 2020</span>)</i></span><span class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<small><a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a></small>)</i></span></div></td></tr></tbody></table>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Latin_numerals_(linguistics)" title="Latin numerals (linguistics)">Latin numerals (linguistics)</a></div>
<p>In ancient times, numbers in Latin were written only with letters. Today, the numbers can be written with the <a href="/wiki/Arabic_numerals" title="Arabic numerals">Arabic numbers</a> as well as with <a href="/wiki/Roman_numerals" title="Roman numerals">Roman numerals</a>. The numbers 1, 2 and 3 and every whole hundred from 200 to 900 are declined as nouns and adjectives, with some differences.
</p>
<table>
<tbody><tr>
<td><span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">ūnus, ūna, ūnum</i></span> (masculine, feminine, neuter)
</td>

<td>one

<p>The numbers from 4 to 100 do not change their endings. As in modern descendants such as Spanish, the gender for naming a number in isolation is masculine, so that "1, 2, 3" is counted as <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">ūnus, duo, trēs</i></span>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Example_text">Example text</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Latin&amp;action=edit&amp;section=38" title="Edit section: Example text">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p><span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la"><a href="/wiki/Commentarii_de_Bello_Gallico" title="Commentarii de Bello Gallico">Commentarii de Bello Gallico</a></i></span>, also called <span title="Latin-language text"><i lang="la">De Bello Gallico</i></span> (<i>The Gallic War</i>), written by <a href="/wiki/Julius_Caesar" title="Julius Caesar">Gaius Julius Caesar</a>, begins with the following passage:
</p>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r996844942">.mw-parser-output .templatequote{overflow:hidden;margin:1em 0;padding:0 40px}.mw-parser-output .templatequote .templatequotecite{line-height:1.5em;text-align:left;padding-left:1.6em;margin-top:0}</style><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Gallia est omnis divisa in partes tres, quarum unam incolunt Belgae, aliam Aquitani, tertiam qui ipsorum lingua Celtae, nostra Galli appellantur. Hi omnes lingua, institutis, legibus inter se differunt. Gallos ab Aquitanis Garumna flumen, a Belgis Matrona et Sequana dividit. Horum omnium fortissimi sunt Belgae, propterea quod a cultu atque humanitate provinciae longissime absunt, minimeque ad eos mercatores saepe commeant atque ea quae ad effeminandos animos pertinent important, proximique sunt Germanis, qui trans Rhenum incolunt, quibuscum continenter bellum gerunt. Qua de causa Helvetii quoque reliquos Gallos virtute praecedunt, quod fere cotidianis proeliis cum Germanis contendunt, cum aut suis finibus eos prohibent aut ipsi in eorum finibus bellum gerunt. Eorum una pars, quam Gallos obtinere dictum est, initium capit a flumine Rhodano, continetur Garumna flumine, Oceano, finibus Belgarum; attingit etiam ab Sequanis et Helvetiis flumen Rhenum; vergit ad septentriones. Belgae ab extremis Galliae finibus oriuntur; pertinent ad inferiorem partem fluminis Rheni; spectant in septentrionem et orientem solem. Aquitania a Garumna flumine ad Pyrenaeos montes et eam partem Oceani quae est ad Hispaniam pertinet; spectat inter occasum solis et septentriones.</p></blockquote>
<p>The same text may be marked for all long vowels (before any possible elisions at word boundary) with <a href="/wiki/Apex_(diacritic)" title="Apex (diacritic)">apices</a> over vowel letters, including customarily before "nf" and "ns" where a long vowel is automatically produced:
</p>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r996844942" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><blockquote class="templatequote"><p>Gallia est omnis dívísa in partés trés, quárum únam incolunt Belgae, aliam Aquítání, tertiam quí ipsórum linguá Celtae, nostrá Gallí appellantur. Hí omnés linguá, ínstitútís, légibus inter sé differunt. Gallós ab Aquítánís Garumna flúmen, á Belgís Mátrona et Séquana dívidit. Hórum omnium fortissimí sunt Belgae, proptereá quod á cultú atque húmánitáte próvinciae longissimé absunt, miniméque ad eós mercátórés saepe commeant atque ea quae ad efféminandós animós pertinent important, proximíque sunt Germánís, quí tráns Rhénum incolunt, quibuscum continenter bellum gerunt. Quá dé causá Helvétií quoque reliquós Gallós virtúte praecédunt, quod feré cotídiánís proeliís cum Germánís contendunt, cum aut suís fínibus eós prohibent aut ipsí in eórum fínibus bellum gerunt. Eórum úna pars, quam Gallós obtinére dictum est, initium capit á flúmine Rhodanó, continétur Garumná flúmine, Óceanó, fínibus Belgárum; attingit etiam ab Séquanís et Helvétiís flúmen Rhénum; vergit ad septentriónés. Belgae ab extrémís Galliae fínibus oriuntur; pertinent ad ínferiórem partem flúminis Rhéní; spectant in septentriónem et orientem sólem. Aquítánia á Garumná flúmine ad Pýrénaeós montés et eam partem Óceaní quae est ad Hispániam pertinet; spectat inter occásum sólis et septentriónés.</p></blockquote>

<li id="cite_note-Sawicka-31"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-Sawicka_31-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Sawicka, Irena. <a class="external text" href="https://ispan.waw.pl/journals/index.php/ch/article/view/ch.2013.006/117" rel="nofollow">"A Crossroad Between West, East and Orient–The Case of Albanian Culture."</a> Colloquia Humanistica. No. 2. Instytut Slawistyki Polskiej Akademii Nauk, 2013. Page 97: "Even according to Albanian linguists, Albanian vocabulary is composed in 60 percent of Latin words from different periods... When albanological studies were just emerging, it happened that Albanian was classified as a Romance language. Already there exists the idea of a common origin of both Albanian and Rumanian languages. The Rumanian grammar is almost identical to that of Albanian, but it may be as well the effect of later convergence within the Balkan Sprachbund.."</span>

<li id="cite_note-45"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-45">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Who only knows Latin can go across the whole Poland from one side to the other one just like he was at his own home, just like he was born there.
